      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
        <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
         <ul>
          <li class="nv-view">
           <a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Palestine" title="Template:History of Palestine">
            <span title="View this template">
             v
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-talk">
           <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Palestine" title="Template talk:History of Palestine">
            <span title="Discuss this template">
             t
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-edit">
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Palestine&amp;action=edit">
            <span title="Edit this template">
             e
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Latin Kingdom of Jerusalem
      </b>
      was a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crusader_state" title="Crusader state">
       crusader state
      </a>
      established in the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Levant" title="Southern Levant">
       Southern Levant
      </a>
      in 1099 after the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">
       First Crusade
      </a>
      . The kingdom lasted nearly two hundred years, from 1099 until 1291 when the last remaining possession,
      <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">
       Acre
      </a>
      , was destroyed by the
      <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">
       Mamluks
      </a>
      , but its history is divided into two distinct periods. The sometimes so-called
      <b>
       First Kingdom of Jerusalem
      </b>
      lasted from 1099 to 1187, when it was almost entirely overrun by
      <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">
       Saladin
      </a>
      . After the subsequent
      <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">
       Third Crusade
      </a>
      , the kingdom was re-established in Acre in 1192, and lasted until that city's destruction in 1291. This second kingdom is sometimes called the
      <b>
       Second Kingdom of Jerusalem
      </b>
      or the
      <b>
       Kingdom of Acre
      </b>
      , after its new capital.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_boundaries">
       Geographic boundaries
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geographic boundaries">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      At first the kingdom was little more than a loose collection of towns and cities captured during the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crusade" title="Crusade">
       crusade
      </a>
      , but at its height in the mid-12th century the kingdom roughly encompassed the territory of modern-day
      <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
       Israel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Palestine" title="Palestine">
       Palestine
      </a>
      and the southern parts of
      <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">
       Lebanon
      </a>
      . From the
      <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">
       Mediterranean Sea
      </a>
      , the kingdom extended in a thin strip of land from
      <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">
       Beirut
      </a>
      in the north to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinai_Desert" title="Sinai Desert">
       Sinai Desert
      </a>
      in the south; into modern
      <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">
       Jordan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      in the east, and towards
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid" title="Fatimid">
       Fatimid
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      in the west. Three other
      <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">
       crusader states
      </a>
      founded during and after the First Crusade were located further north: the
      <a href="/wiki/County_of_Edessa" title="County of Edessa">
       County of Edessa
      </a>
      (10971144), the
      <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Antioch" title="Principality of Antioch">
       Principality of Antioch
      </a>
      (10981268), and the
      <a href="/wiki/County_of_Tripoli" title="County of Tripoli">
       County of Tripoli
      </a>
      (11091289). While all three were independent, they were closely tied to Jerusalem. Beyond these to the north and east lay the states of
      <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Kingdom_of_Cilicia" title="Armenian Kingdom of Cilicia">
       Armenian Cilicia
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
       Byzantine Empire
      </a>
      , with which Jerusalem had a close relationship in the twelfth century. Further east, various Muslim emirates were located which were ultimately allied with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid" title="Abbasid">
       Abbasid
      </a>
      caliph in
      <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">
       Baghdad
      </a>
      . The fragmentation of the Muslim east allowed for the initial success of the crusade, but as the 12th century progressed, the kingdom's Muslim neighbours were united by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nur_ad-Din_Zangi" title="Nur ad-Din Zangi">
       Nur ad-Din Zangi
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">
       Saladin
      </a>
      , who vigorously began to recapture lost territory.
      <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
       Jerusalem
      </a>
      itself fell to Saladin in 1187, and in the 13th century the kingdom was reduced to a few cities along the Mediterranean coast. In this period, the kingdom was ruled by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lusignan" title="Lusignan">
       Lusignan
      </a>
      dynasty of the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Cyprus" title="Kingdom of Cyprus">
       Kingdom of Cyprus
      </a>
      , another crusader state founded during the Third Crusade. Dynastic ties also strengthened with Tripoli, Antioch, and Armenia. The kingdom was soon increasingly dominated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Italian_city-states" title="Italian city-states">
       Italian city-states
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">
       Venice
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">
       Genoa
      </a>
      , as well as the imperial ambitions of the
      <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">
       Holy Roman Emperors
      </a>
      . Emperor
      <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">
       Frederick II
      </a>
      (reigned 1220-1250) claimed the kingdom by marriage, but his presence sparked
      <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Lombards" title="War of the Lombards">
       a civil war
      </a>
      (1228-1243) among the kingdom's nobility. The kingdom became little more than a pawn in the politics and warfare of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayyubid" title="Ayyubid">
       Ayyubid
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">
       Mamluk
      </a>
      dynasties in Egypt, as well as the
      <a href="/wiki/Khwarazmian_dynasty" title="Khwarazmian dynasty">
       Khwarezmian
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol" title="Mongol">
       Mongol
      </a>
      invaders. As a relatively minor kingdom, it received little financial or military support from Europe; despite numerous small expeditions, Europeans generally proved unwilling to undertake an expensive journey to the east for an apparently losing cause. The Mamluk sultans
      <a href="/wiki/Baibars" title="Baibars">
       Baibars
      </a>
      (reigned 1260-1277) and
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Ashraf_Khalil" title="Al-Ashraf Khalil">
       al-Ashraf Khalil
      </a>
      (reigned 1290-1293) eventually reconquered all the remaining crusader strongholds, culminating in the
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Acre_(1291)" title="Siege of Acre (1291)">
       destruction of Acre
      </a>
      in 1291.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="People">
       People
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: People">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The kingdom was ethnically, religiously, and linguistically diverse, although the crusaders themselves and their descendants were an elite Catholic minority. They imported many customs and institutions from their homelands in Western Europe, and there were close familial and political connections with the West throughout the kingdom's existence. The kingdom also inherited "oriental" qualities, influenced by the pre-existing customs and populations. The majority of the kingdom's inhabitants were native Christians, especially
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox" title="Greek Orthodox">
       Greek
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Orthodox" title="Syrian Orthodox">
       Syrian Orthodox
      </a>
      , as well as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">
       Sunni
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%27a" title="Shi'a">
       Shi'a
      </a>
      Muslims. There were also a small number of
      <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
       Jews
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">
       Samaritans
      </a>
      . The native Christians and Muslims, who were a marginalized lower class, tended to speak Greek and Arabic, while the crusaders spoke French, and other Western European languages.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Jerusalem" title="Timeline of Jerusalem">
       Timeline of Jerusalem
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="First_Crusade_and_the_foundation_of_the_kingdom">
       First Crusade and the foundation of the kingdom
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: First Crusade and the foundation of the kingdom">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">
       First Crusade
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The First Crusade was preached at the
      <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Clermont" title="Council of Clermont">
       Council of Clermont
      </a>
      in 1095 by
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">
       Pope Urban II
      </a>
      , with the goal of assisting the
      <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
       Byzantine Empire
      </a>
      against the invasions of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuk_Turks" title="Seljuk Turks">
       Seljuk Turks
      </a>
      . However, the main objective quickly became the control of the
      <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">
       Holy Land
      </a>
      . The Byzantines were frequently at war with the Seljuqs and other Turkish dynasties for control of
      <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">
       Anatolia
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      . The Sunni Seljuqs had formerly ruled the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Seljuq_Empire" title="Great Seljuq Empire">
       Great Seljuq Empire
      </a>
      , but this empire had collapsed into several smaller states after the death of
      <a href="/wiki/Malik-Shah_I" title="Malik-Shah I">
       Malik-Shah I
      </a>
      in 1092. Malik-Shah was succeeded in the Anatolian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_R%C3%BBm" title="Sultanate of Rm">
       Sultanate of Rm
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Kilij_Arslan_I" title="Kilij Arslan I">
       Kilij Arslan I
      </a>
      , and in Syria by his brother
      <a href="/wiki/Tutush_I" title="Tutush I">
       Tutush I
      </a>
      , who died in 1095. Tutush's sons
      <a href="/wiki/Fakhr_al-Mulk_Radwan" title="Fakhr al-Mulk Radwan">
       Fakhr al-Mulk Radwan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Duqaq" title="Duqaq">
       Duqaq
      </a>
      inherited
      <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">
       Aleppo
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">
       Damascus
      </a>
      respectively, further dividing Syria amongst emirs antagonistic towards each other, as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Kerbogha" title="Kerbogha">
       Kerbogha
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Atabeg" title="Atabeg">
       atabeg
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">
       Mosul
      </a>
      . This disunity among the Anatolian and Syrian emirs allowed the crusaders to overcome any military opposition they faced on the way to Jerusalem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Egypt and much of Palestine were controlled by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_people" title="Arab people">
       Arab
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">
       Shi'ite
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">
       Fatimid Caliphate
      </a>
      , which had extended further into Syria before the arrival of the Seljuqs. Warfare between the Fatimids and Seljuqs caused great disruption for the local Christians and for western pilgrims. The Fatimids, under the nominal rule of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">
       caliph
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Musta%27li" title="Al-Musta'li">
       al-Musta'li
      </a>
      but actually controlled by
      <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">
       vizier
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Afdal_Shahanshah" title="Al-Afdal Shahanshah">
       al-Afdal Shahanshah
      </a>
      , had lost Jerusalem to the Seljuqs in 1073;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      they recaptured it in 1098 from the
      <a href="/wiki/Artuqids" title="Artuqids">
       Artuqids
      </a>
      , a smaller Turkish tribe associated with the Seljuqs, just before the arrival of the crusaders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Godefroi1099.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="301" data-file-width="355" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Godefroi1099.jpg/220px-Godefroi1099.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Godefroi1099.jpg/330px-Godefroi1099.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Godefroi1099.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Godefroi1099.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        After the successful siege of Jerusalem in 1099,
        <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_of_Bouillon" title="Godfrey of Bouillon">
         Godfrey of Bouillon
        </a>
        , leader of the First Crusade, became the first ruler of the Kingdom of Jerusalem.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The crusaders arrived at Jerusalem in June 1099; a few of the neighbouring towns (
      <a href="/wiki/Ramla" title="Ramla">
       Ramla
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lod" title="Lod">
       Lydda
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">
       Bethlehem
      </a>
      , and others) were taken first, and Jerusalem itself
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(1099)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (1099)">
       was captured
      </a>
      on July 15.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 22 July, a council was held in the
      <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">
       Church of the Holy Sepulchre
      </a>
      to establish a king for the newly created Kingdom of Jerusalem.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raymond_IV_of_Toulouse" title="Raymond IV of Toulouse">
       Raymond IV of Toulouse
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_of_Bouillon" title="Godfrey of Bouillon">
       Godfrey of Bouillon
      </a>
      were recognized as the leaders of the crusade and the siege of Jerusalem. Raymond was the wealthier and more powerful of the two, but at first he refused to become king, perhaps attempting to show his piety and probably hoping that the other nobles would insist upon his election anyway.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyerman159_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tyerman159-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The more popular Godfrey did not hesitate like Raymond, and accepted a position as secular leader. Although it is widely claimed that he took the title
      <i>
       Advocatus Sancti Sepulchri
      </i>
      ("advocate" or "defender" of the Holy Sepulchre), this title is only used in a letter that was not written by Godfrey. Instead, Godfrey himself seems to have used the more ambiguous term
      <i>
       princeps
      </i>
      , or simply retained his title of
      <i>
       dux
      </i>
      from Lower Lorraine. According to William of Tyre, writing in the later 12th century when Godfrey had become a legendary hero, he refused to wear "a crown of gold" where Christ had worn "a crown of thorns".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_the_Monk" title="Robert the Monk">
       Robert the Monk
      </a>
      is the only contemporary chronicler of the crusade to report that Godfrey took the title "king".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Raymond was incensed and took his army to forage away from the city. The new kingdom, and Godfrey's reputation, was secured with the defeat of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid" title="Fatimid">
       Fatimid
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egyptian
      </a>
      army under
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Afdal_Shahanshah" title="Al-Afdal Shahanshah">
       al-Afdal Shahanshah
      </a>
      at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ascalon" title="Battle of Ascalon">
       Battle of Ascalon
      </a>
      one month after the conquest, on August 12, but Raymond and Godfrey's continued antagonism prevented the crusaders from taking control of
      <a href="/wiki/Ashkelon" title="Ashkelon">
       Ascalon
      </a>
      itself.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There was still some uncertainty about what to do with the new kingdom. The
      <a href="/wiki/Papal_legate" title="Papal legate">
       papal legate
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daimbert_of_Pisa" title="Daimbert of Pisa">
       Daimbert of Pisa
      </a>
      convinced Godfrey to hand over Jerusalem to him as
      <a href="/wiki/Latin_Patriarch_of_Jerusalem" title="Latin Patriarch of Jerusalem">
       Latin Patriarch
      </a>
      , with the intention to set up a theocratic state directly under papal control. According to William of Tyre, Godfrey may have supported Daimbert's efforts, and he agreed to take possession of "one or two other cities and thus enlarge the kingdom" if Daimbert were permitted to rule Jerusalem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Godfrey did indeed increase the boundaries of the kingdom, by capturing
      <a href="/wiki/Jaffa" title="Jaffa">
       Jaffa
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Haifa" title="Haifa">
       Haifa
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tiberias" title="Tiberias">
       Tiberias
      </a>
      , and other cities, and reducing many others to tributary status. He set the foundations for the
      <a href="/wiki/Vassals_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Vassals of the Kingdom of Jerusalem">
       system of vassalage
      </a>
      in the kingdom, establishing the
      <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Galilee" title="Principality of Galilee">
       Principality of Galilee
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/County_of_Jaffa" title="County of Jaffa">
       County of Jaffa
      </a>
      . But his reign was short, and he died of an illness in 1100. His brother
      <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_I_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin I of Jerusalem">
       Baldwin of Boulogne
      </a>
      successfully outmanoeuvred Daimbert and claimed Jerusalem for himself as "
      <a href="/wiki/King_of_Jerusalem" title="King of Jerusalem">
       king of the Latins of Jerusalem
      </a>
      ". Daimbert compromised by crowning Baldwin in Bethlehem rather than Jerusalem, but the path for a secular state had been laid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Within this secular framework, a
      <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
       Catholic church
      </a>
      hierarchy was established, overtop of the local
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">
       Eastern Orthodox
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Orthodox" title="Syrian Orthodox">
       Syrian Orthodox
      </a>
      authorities, who retained their own hierarchies (the Catholics considered them schismatics and thus illegitimate; and vice versa). Under the Latin Patriarch there were four suffragan archdioceses and numerous dioceses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion">
       Expansion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Expansion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      During Baldwin I's reign the kingdom expanded even further. The numbers of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_people#Middle_Ages" title="Latin people">
       Latin
      </a>
      inhabitants increased, as the minor
      <a href="/wiki/Crusade_of_1101" title="Crusade of 1101">
       crusade of 1101
      </a>
      brought reinforcements to the kingdom. Baldwin repopulated Jerusalem with Franks and native Christians, after his expedition
      <a href="/wiki/Oultrejordain" title="Oultrejordain">
       across the Jordan
      </a>
      in 1115.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With help from the Italian city-states and other adventurers, notably King
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sigurd_I_of_Norway" title="Sigurd I of Norway">
       Sigurd I of Norway
      </a>
      , Baldwin captured the port cities of Acre (1104),
      <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">
       Beirut
      </a>
      (1110), and
      <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">
       Sidon
      </a>
      (1111), while exerting his
      <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">
       suzerainty
      </a>
      over the other
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crusader_state" title="Crusader state">
       crusader states
      </a>
      to the north  Edessa (which he had founded in 1097 during the crusade), Antioch, and Tripoli, which he helped capture in 1109. He successfully defended against Muslim invasions, from the Fatimids at the numerous
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ramla_(disambiguation)" title="Battle of Ramla (disambiguation)">
       battles at Ramla
      </a>
      and elsewhere in the southwest of the kingdom, and from
      <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">
       Damascus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">
       Mosul
      </a>
      at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_al-Sannabra" title="Battle of al-Sannabra">
       Battle of al-Sannabra
      </a>
      in the northeast in 1113.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Madden" title="Thomas Madden">
       Thomas Madden
      </a>
      says, Baldwin was "the true founder of the kingdom of Jerusalem", who "had transformed a tenuous arrangement into a solid feudal state. With brilliance and diligence, he established a strong monarchy, conquered the Palestinian coast, reconciled the crusader barons, and built strong frontiers against the kingdom's Muslim neighbours."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Funeral_of_Baldwin_I.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="908" data-file-width="1024" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Funeral_of_Baldwin_I.jpg/220px-Funeral_of_Baldwin_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Funeral_of_Baldwin_I.jpg/330px-Funeral_of_Baldwin_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Funeral_of_Baldwin_I.jpg/440px-Funeral_of_Baldwin_I.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Funeral_of_Baldwin_I.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The funeral of
        <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_I_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin I of Jerusalem">
         Baldwin I
        </a>
        from the book:
        <i>
         Les Passages d'outremer faits par les Franais contre les Turcs depuis Charlemagne jusqu'en 1462
        </i>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Baldwin brought with him an Armenian wife, traditionally named
      <a href="/wiki/Arda_of_Armenia" title="Arda of Armenia">
       Arda
      </a>
      (although never named such by contemporaries), whom he had married to gain political support from the Armenian population in Edessa, and whom he quickly set aside when he no longer needed Armenian support in Jerusalem. He bigamously married
      <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_del_Vasto" title="Adelaide del Vasto">
       Adelaide del Vasto
      </a>
      , regent of Sicily, in 1113, but was convinced to divorce her as well in 1117; Adelaide's son from her first marriage,
      <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">
       Roger II of Sicily
      </a>
      , never forgave Jerusalem, and for decades withheld much-needed Sicilian naval support.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Baldwin died without heirs in 1118, during a campaign against Egypt, and the kingdom was offered to his brother
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eustace_III_of_Boulogne" title="Eustace III of Boulogne">
       Eustace III of Boulogne
      </a>
      , who had accompanied Baldwin and Godfrey on the crusade. Eustace was uninterested, and instead the crown passed to Baldwin's relative, probably a cousin,
      <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_II_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin II of Jerusalem">
       Baldwin of Le Bourg
      </a>
      , who had previously succeeded him in Edessa. Baldwin II was an able ruler, and he too successfully defended against Fatimid and Seljuk invasions. Although Antioch was severely weakened after the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ager_Sanguinis" title="Battle of Ager Sanguinis">
       Battle of Ager Sanguinis
      </a>
      in 1119, and Baldwin himself was held captive by the emir of Aleppo from 11221124, Baldwin led the crusader states to victory at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Azaz_(1125)" title="Battle of Azaz (1125)">
       Battle of Azaz
      </a>
      in 1125. His reign saw the establishment of the first
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_order_(society)" title="Military order (society)">
       military orders
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Knights_Hospitaller" title="Knights Hospitaller">
       Knights Hospitaller
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Knights_Templar" title="Knights Templar">
       Knights Templar
      </a>
      ; the earliest surviving written laws of the kingdom, compiled at the
      <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Nablus" title="Council of Nablus">
       Council of Nablus
      </a>
      in 1120; and the first commercial treaty with
      <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">
       Venice
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Pactum_Warmundi" title="Pactum Warmundi">
       Pactum Warmundi
      </a>
      , in 1124. The increase of naval and military support from Venice led to the capture of
      <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">
       Tyre
      </a>
      that year. The influence of Jerusalem was further extended over Edessa and Antioch, where Baldwin II acted as regent when their own leaders were killed in battle, although there were regency governments in Jerusalem as well during Baldwin's captivity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Baldwin was married to the Armenian noblewoman
      <a href="/wiki/Morphia_of_Melitene" title="Morphia of Melitene">
       Morphia of Melitene
      </a>
      , and had four daughters:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hodierna_of_Tripoli" title="Hodierna of Tripoli">
       Hodierna
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Alice_of_Antioch" title="Alice of Antioch">
       Alice
      </a>
      , who married into the families of the Count of Tripoli and Prince of Antioch;
      <a href="/wiki/Ioveta_of_Bethany" title="Ioveta of Bethany">
       Ioveta
      </a>
      , who became an influential abbess; and the eldest,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melisende_of_Jerusalem" title="Melisende of Jerusalem">
       Melisende
      </a>
      , who was his heir and succeeded him upon his death in 1131, with her husband
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulk_of_Jerusalem" title="Fulk of Jerusalem">
       Fulk V of Anjou
      </a>
      as king-consort. Their son, the future
      <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_III_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin III of Jerusalem">
       Baldwin III
      </a>
      , was named co-heir by his grandfather.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Edessa.2C_Damascus.2C_and_the_Second_Crusade">
       Edessa, Damascus, and the Second Crusade
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Edessa, Damascus, and the Second Crusade">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">
       Second Crusade
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crois%C3%A9s.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="434" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Crois%C3%A9s.jpg/220px-Crois%C3%A9s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Crois%C3%A9s.jpg/330px-Crois%C3%A9s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4e/Crois%C3%A9s.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crois%C3%A9s.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Crusader warriors
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Fulk was an experienced
      <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">
       crusader
      </a>
      , who had brought military support to the kingdom during a pilgrimage in 1120. He brought Jerusalem into the sphere of the
      <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">
       Angevin Empire
      </a>
      , as the father of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geoffrey_V_of_Anjou" title="Geoffrey V of Anjou">
       Geoffrey V of Anjou
      </a>
      and grandfather of the future
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">
       Henry II of England
      </a>
      . Not everyone appreciated the imposition of a foreigner as king. In 1132 Antioch, Tripoli, and Edessa all asserted their independence and conspired to prevent Fulk from exercising the suzerainty of Jerusalem over them. He defeated Tripoli in battle, and settled the regency in Antioch by arranging a marriage between the countess, Melisende's niece
      <a href="/wiki/Constance_of_Antioch" title="Constance of Antioch">
       Constance
      </a>
      , and his own relative
      <a href="/wiki/Raymond_of_Poitiers" title="Raymond of Poitiers">
       Raymond of Poitiers
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Meanwhile, in Jerusalem, the native crusader nobles opposed Fulk's preference for his Angevin retinue. In 1134
      <a href="/wiki/Hugh_II_of_Jaffa" title="Hugh II of Jaffa">
       Hugh II of Jaffa
      </a>
      revolted against Fulk, allying with the Muslim garrison at Ascalon, for which he was convicted of treason
      <i>
       in absentia
      </i>
      . The Latin Patriarch intervened to settle the dispute, but an assassination attempt was then made on Hugh, for which Fulk was blamed. This scandal allowed Melisende and her supporters to gain control of the government, just as her father had intended.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Accordingly, Fulk "became so uxorious that...not even in unimportant cases did he take any measures without her knowledge and assistance."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Fulk was then faced with a new and more dangerous enemy: the atabeg
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zengi" title="Zengi">
       Zengi
      </a>
      of Mosul, who had taken control of
      <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">
       Aleppo
      </a>
      and had set his sights on
      <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">
       Damascus
      </a>
      as well; the union of these three states would have been a serious blow to the growing power of Jerusalem. A brief intervention in 11371138 by the Byzantine emperor
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_II_Comnenus" title="John II Comnenus">
       John II Comnenus
      </a>
      , who wished to assert imperial suzerainty over all the crusader states, did nothing to stop the threat of Zengi; in 1139 Damascus and Jerusalem recognized the severity of the threat to both states, and an alliance was concluded which halted Zengi's advance. Fulk used this time to construct numerous castles, including
      <a href="/wiki/Ibelin" title="Ibelin">
       Ibelin
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerak" title="Kerak">
       Kerak
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the death of both Fulk and Emperor John in separate hunting accidents in 1143, Zengi invaded and
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Edessa" title="Siege of Edessa">
       conquered Edessa
      </a>
      in 1144. Queen Melisende, now regent for her elder son Baldwin III, appointed a new constable,
      <a href="/wiki/Manasses_of_Hierges" title="Manasses of Hierges">
       Manasses of Hierges
      </a>
      , to head the army after Fulk's death, but Edessa could not be recaptured, despite Zengi's own assassination in 1146.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The fall of Edessa shocked Europe, and a
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">
       Second Crusade
      </a>
      arrived in 1148.
     </p>
     <p>
      After
      <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Acre" title="Council of Acre">
       meeting in Acre
      </a>
      in June, the crusading kings
      <a href="/wiki/Louis_VII_of_France" title="Louis VII of France">
       Louis VII of France
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Conrad_III_of_Germany" title="Conrad III of Germany">
       Conrad III of Germany
      </a>
      agreed with Melisende, Baldwin III and the major nobles of the kingdom to attack
      <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">
       Damascus
      </a>
      . Zengi's territory had been divided amongst his sons after his death, and Damascus no longer felt threatened, so an alliance had been made with Zengi's son
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nur_ad-Din_Zangi" title="Nur ad-Din Zangi">
       Nur ad-Din
      </a>
      , the emir of Aleppo. Perhaps remembering attacks launched on Jerusalem from Damascus in previous decades, Damascus seemed to be the best target for the crusade, rather than Aleppo or another city to the north which would have allowed for the recapture of Edessa. The subsequent
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Damascus_(1148)" title="Siege of Damascus (1148)">
       Siege of Damascus
      </a>
      was a complete failure; when the city seemed to be on the verge of collapse, the crusader army suddenly moved against another section of the walls, and were driven back. The crusaders retreated within three days. There were rumours of treachery and bribery, and Conrad III felt betrayed by the nobility of Jerusalem. Whatever the reason for the failure, the French and German armies returned home, and a few years later Damascus was firmly under Nur ad-Din's control.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war">
       Civil war
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Civil war">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The failure of the Second Crusade had dire long-term consequences for the kingdom. The West was hesitant to send large-scale expeditions; for the next few decades, only small armies came, headed by minor European nobles who desired to make a pilgrimage. The Muslim states of Syria were meanwhile gradually united by Nur ad-Din, who defeated the Principality of Antioch at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Inab" title="Battle of Inab">
       Battle of Inab
      </a>
      in 1149 and gained control of Damascus in 1154. Nur ad-Din was extremely pious and during his rule the concept of
      <a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">
       jihad
      </a>
      came to be interpreted as a kind of counter-crusade against the kingdom, which was an impediment to Muslim unity, both political and spiritual.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tower_of_david_jerusalem.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Tower_of_david_jerusalem.jpg/220px-Tower_of_david_jerusalem.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Tower_of_david_jerusalem.jpg/330px-Tower_of_david_jerusalem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Tower_of_david_jerusalem.jpg/440px-Tower_of_david_jerusalem.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tower_of_david_jerusalem.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_David" title="Tower of David">
         Tower of David
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
         Jerusalem
        </a>
        as it appears today
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In Jerusalem, the crusaders were distracted by a conflict between Melisende and Baldwin III. Melisende continued to rule as regent long after Baldwin came of age. She was supported by, among others, Manasses of Hierges, who essentially governed for her as constable; her son
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amalric_I_of_Jerusalem" title="Amalric I of Jerusalem">
       Amalric
      </a>
      , whom she set up as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Count_of_Jaffa" title="Count of Jaffa">
       Count of Jaffa
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Milly" title="Philip of Milly">
       Philip of Milly
      </a>
      ; and the
      <a href="/wiki/Ibelin" title="Ibelin">
       Ibelin
      </a>
      family. Baldwin asserted his independence by mediating disputes in Antioch and Tripoli, and gained the support of the Ibelin brothers when they began to oppose Manasses' growing power, thanks to his marriage to their widowed mother
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Helvis_of_Ramla&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Helvis of Ramla (page does not exist)">
       Helvis of Ramla
      </a>
      . In 1153 Baldwin had himself crowned as sole ruler, and a compromise was reached by which the kingdom was divided in two, with Baldwin taking Acre and Tyre in the north and Melisende remaining in control of Jerusalem and the cities of the south. Baldwin was able to replace Manasses with one of his own supporters,
      <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_II_of_Toron" title="Humphrey II of Toron">
       Humphrey II of Toron
      </a>
      . Baldwin and Melisende knew that this situation was untenable. Baldwin soon invaded his mother's possessions, defeated Manasses, and besieged his mother in the
      <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_David" title="Tower of David">
       Tower of David
      </a>
      in Jerusalem. Melisende surrendered and retired to Nablus, but Baldwin appointed her his regent and chief advisor, and she retained some of her influence, especially in appointing ecclesiastical officials.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1153, Baldwin launched
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ascalon_(1153)" title="Battle of Ascalon (1153)">
       an offensive against Ascalon
      </a>
      , the fortress in the south from which Fatimid Egyptian armies had continually raided Jerusalem since the foundation of the kingdom. The fortress was captured and was added to the County of Jaffa, still in the possession of his brother Amalric.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantine_alliance_and_invasion_of_Egypt">
       Byzantine alliance and invasion of Egypt
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Byzantine alliance and invasion of Egypt">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manuelcomnenus.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="175" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/Manuelcomnenus.jpg" width="175"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manuelcomnenus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Emperor" title="Byzantine Emperor">
         Byzantine Emperor
        </a>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Comnenus" title="Manuel I Comnenus">
         Manuel I Comnenus
        </a>
        , who became a close ally of the Kingdom of Jerusalem.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      With the capture of Ascalon the southern border of the kingdom was now secure, and Egypt, formerly a major threat to the kingdom but now destabilized under the reign of several underaged caliphs, was reduced to a tributary state. Nur ad-Din remained a threat in the east, and Baldwin had to contend with the advances of Byzantine emperor
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Comnenus" title="Manuel I Comnenus">
       Manuel I Comnenus
      </a>
      , who claimed suzerainty over the Principality of Antioch. In order to bolster the defences of the kingdom against the growing strength of the Muslims, Baldwin III made the first direct alliance with the Byzantine Empire, by marrying
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodora_Comnena" title="Theodora Comnena">
       Theodora Comnena
      </a>
      , a niece of emperor Manuel; Manuel married Baldwin's cousin
      <a href="/wiki/Maria_of_Antioch" title="Maria of Antioch">
       Maria
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As William of Tyre put it, it was hoped that Manuel would be able "to relieve from his own abundance the distress under which our realm was suffering and to change our poverty into superabundance".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When Baldwin died childless in 1162, a year after his mother Melisende, the kingdom passed to his brother Amalric, who renewed the alliance negotiated by Baldwin. In 1163 the chaotic situation in Egypt led to a refusal to pay tribute to Jerusalem, and requests were sent to Nur ad-Din for assistance; in response,
      <a href="/wiki/Crusader_invasions_of_Egypt" title="Crusader invasions of Egypt">
       Amalric invaded
      </a>
      , but was turned back when the Egyptians flooded the Nile at
      <a href="/wiki/Bilbeis" title="Bilbeis">
       Bilbeis
      </a>
      . The Egyptian vizier
      <a href="/wiki/Shawar" title="Shawar">
       Shawar
      </a>
      again requested help from Nur ad-Din, who sent his general
      <a href="/wiki/Shirkuh" title="Shirkuh">
       Shirkuh
      </a>
      , but Shawar quickly turned against him and allied with Amalric. Amalric and Shirkuh both besieged Bilbeis in 1164, but both withdrew due to Nur ad-Din's campaigns against Antioch, where
      <a href="/wiki/Bohemond_III_of_Antioch" title="Bohemond III of Antioch">
       Bohemond III of Antioch
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raymond_III_of_Tripoli" title="Raymond III of Tripoli">
       Raymond III of Tripoli
      </a>
      were defeated at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Harim" title="Battle of Harim">
       Battle of Harim
      </a>
      . It seemed likely that Antioch itself would fall to Nur ad-Din, but he withdrew when Emperor Manuel sent a large Byzantine force to the area. Nur ad-Din sent Shirkuh back to Egypt in 1166, and Shawar again allied with Amalric, who was defeated at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_al-Babein" title="Battle of al-Babein">
       Battle of al-Babein
      </a>
      . Despite the defeat, both sides withdrew, but Shawar remained in control with a crusader garrison in Cairo.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Amalric cemented his alliance with Manuel by marrying Manuel's niece
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maria_Komnene,_Queen_consort_of_Jerusalem" title="Maria Komnene, Queen consort of Jerusalem">
       Maria Komnene
      </a>
      in 1167, and an embassy led by William of Tyre was sent to Constantinople to negotiate a military expedition, but in 1168 Amalric pillaged Bilbeis without waiting for the naval support promised by Manuel. Amalric accomplished nothing else, but his actions prompted Shawar to switch sides again and seek help from Shirkuh. Shawar was promptly assassinated, and when Shirkuh died in 1169, he was succeeded by his nephew Yusuf, better known as
      <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">
       Saladin
      </a>
      . That year, Manuel sent a large Byzantine fleet of some 300 ships to assist Amalric, and the town of
      <a href="/wiki/Damietta" title="Damietta">
       Damietta
      </a>
      was placed under siege. However, the Byzantine fleet sailed with enough provisions for only three months. By the time that the crusaders were ready supplies were already running out and the fleet retired. Each side sought to blame the other for the failure, but both knew that they could not take Egypt without the other's assistance: the alliance was maintained, and plans for another campaign in Egypt were made, which ultimately were to come to naught.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the end, Nur ad-Din was victorious and Saladin established himself as
      <a href="/wiki/Sultan_of_Egypt" title="Sultan of Egypt">
       Sultan of Egypt
      </a>
      . Saladin soon began to assert his independence from Nur ad-Din, and with the death of both Amalric and Nur ad-Din in 1174, he was well-placed to begin exerting control over Nur ad-Din's Syrian possessions as well.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Upon the death of the pro-western Emperor Manuel in 1180, the Kingdom of Jerusalem lost its most powerful ally.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Baldwin_IV_and_the_succession_crisis">
       Baldwin IV and the succession crisis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Baldwin IV and the succession crisis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Amalric was succeeded by his young son,
      <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_IV_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin IV of Jerusalem">
       Baldwin IV
      </a>
      , who was discovered at a very young age to be a
      <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">
       leper
      </a>
      . The subsequent events have often been interpreted as a struggle between two opposing factions, the "court party", made up of Baldwin's mother, Amalric's first wife
      <a href="/wiki/Agnes_of_Courtenay" title="Agnes of Courtenay">
       Agnes of Courtenay
      </a>
      , her
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Courtenay" title="House of Courtenay">
       immediate family
      </a>
      , and recent arrivals from Europe who were inexperienced in the affairs of the kingdom and who were in favour of war with Saladin; and the "noble party", led by Raymond of Tripoli and the lesser nobility of the kingdom, who favoured peaceful co-existence with the Muslims. This is the interpretation offered by William of Tyre, who was firmly placed in the "noble" camp, and his view was taken up by subsequent historians; in the 20th century,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Marshall_W._Baldwin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Marshall W. Baldwin (page does not exist)">
       Marshall W. Baldwin
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Steven_Runciman" title="Steven Runciman">
       Steven Runciman
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hans_E._Mayer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hans E. Mayer (page does not exist)">
       Hans E. Mayer
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      favoured this interpretation.
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_W._Edbury&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peter W. Edbury (page does not exist)">
       Peter W. Edbury
      </a>
      , on the other hand, argues that William, as well as the thirteenth-century authors who continued William's chronicle in French and were allied to Raymond's supporters in the Ibelin family, cannot be considered impartial.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although the events were clearly a dynastic struggle, "the division was not between native barons and newcomers from the West, but between the king's maternal and paternal kin."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Miles_of_Plancy" title="Miles of Plancy">
       Miles of Plancy
      </a>
      was briefly
      <a href="/wiki/Officers_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem#Bailiffs" title="Officers of the Kingdom of Jerusalem">
       <i>
        bailli
       </i>
      </a>
      or regent during Baldwin IV's minority. Miles was assassinated in October 1174, and Count
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raymond_III_of_Tripoli" title="Raymond III of Tripoli">
       Raymond III of Tripoli
      </a>
      , Amalric's first cousin, became regent. It is highly probable that Raymond or his supporters engineered the assassination.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Baldwin reached his majority in 1176, and despite his illness he no longer had any legal need for a regent. Since Raymond was his nearest relative in the male line with a strong claim to the throne, there was concern about the extent of his ambitions, although he had no direct heirs of his own. To balance this, the king turned from time to time to his uncle,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joscelin_III_of_Edessa" title="Joscelin III of Edessa">
       Joscelin III of Edessa
      </a>
      , who was appointed seneschal in 1176; Joscelin was more closely related to Baldwin than Raymond was, but had no claim to the throne himself.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As a leper, Baldwin could have no children and could not be expected to rule much longer, so the focus of his succession passed to his sister
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sibylla_of_Jerusalem" title="Sibylla of Jerusalem">
       Sibylla
      </a>
      and his younger half-sister
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isabella_of_Jerusalem" title="Isabella of Jerusalem">
       Isabella
      </a>
      . Baldwin and his advisors recognised that it was essential for Sibylla to be married to a Western nobleman in order to access support from Europe in a military crisis; while Raymond was still regent, a marriage was arranged for Sibylla and
      <a href="/wiki/William_of_Montferrat,_Count_of_Jaffa_and_Ascalon" title="William of Montferrat, Count of Jaffa and Ascalon">
       William of Montferrat
      </a>
      , a cousin of Louis VII of France and of
      <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">
       Frederick Barbarossa, Holy Roman Emperor
      </a>
      . It was hoped that by allying with a relative of the western emperor, Frederick would come to the kingdom's aid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jerusalem looked again towards the Byzantine Empire for help, and Emperor Manuel was looking for a way to restore his empire's prestige after his defeat at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Myriokephalon" title="Battle of Myriokephalon">
       Battle of Myriokephalon
      </a>
      in 1176; this mission was undertaken by
      <a href="/wiki/Raynald_of_Ch%C3%A2tillon" title="Raynald of Chtillon">
       Raynald of Chtillon
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After William of Montferrat arrived in 1176, he fell ill and died in June 1177, leaving Sibylla widowed and pregnant with the future
      <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_V_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin V of Jerusalem">
       Baldwin V
      </a>
      . Raynald was then named regent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Soon afterwards,
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_I,_Count_of_Flanders" title="Philip I, Count of Flanders">
       Philip of Flanders
      </a>
      arrived in Jerusalem on pilgrimage; he was Baldwin IV's cousin, and the king offered him the regency and command of the army, both of which Philip refused, although he objected to the appointment of Raynald as regent. Philip then attempted to intervene in the negotiations for Sibylla's second husband, and suggested one of his own retinue, but the native barons refused his suggestion. In addition, Philip seemed to think he could carve out a territory of his own in Egypt, but he refused to participate with the planned Byzantine-Jerusalem expedition. The expedition was delayed and finally cancelled, and Philip took his army away to the north.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most of the army of Jerusalem marched north with Philip, Raymond III, and Bohemond III to attack
      <a href="/wiki/Hama" title="Hama">
       Hama
      </a>
      , and Saladin took the opportunity to invade the kingdom. Baldwin proved to be an effective and energetic king as well as being a brilliant military commander: he defeated Saladin at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Montgisard" title="Battle of Montgisard">
       Battle of Montgisard
      </a>
      in September 1177 despite being greatly outnumbered and having to rely on a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levee-en-masse" title="Levee-en-masse">
       levee-en-masse
      </a>
      . Although Baldwin's presence despite his illness was inspirational, direct military decisions were actually made by Raynald.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugh_III_of_Burgundy" title="Hugh III of Burgundy">
       Hugh III of Burgundy
      </a>
      was expected to come to Jerusalem and marry Sibylla, but Hugh was unable to leave France due to the political unrest there in 11791180 following the death of Louis VII. Meanwhile, Baldwin IV's stepmother Maria, mother of Isabella and stepmother of Sibylla, married
      <a href="/wiki/Balian_of_Ibelin" title="Balian of Ibelin">
       Balian of Ibelin
      </a>
      . At Easter in 1180, Raymond and his cousin
      <a href="/wiki/Bohemond_III_of_Antioch" title="Bohemond III of Antioch">
       Bohemond III of Antioch
      </a>
      attempted to force Sibylla to marry Balian's brother
      <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_of_Ibelin" title="Baldwin of Ibelin">
       Baldwin of Ibelin
      </a>
      . Raymond and Bohemond were King Baldwin's nearest male relatives in the paternal line, and could have claimed the throne if the king died without an heir or a suitable replacement. Before Raymond and Bohemond arrived, Agnes and King Baldwin arranged for Sibylla to be married to a
      <a href="/wiki/Poitou" title="Poitou">
       Poitevin
      </a>
      newcomer,
      <a href="/wiki/Guy_of_Lusignan" title="Guy of Lusignan">
       Guy of Lusignan
      </a>
      , whose older brother
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amalric_II_of_Jerusalem" title="Amalric II of Jerusalem">
       Amalric of Lusignan
      </a>
      was already an established figure at court.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Internationally, the Lusignans were useful as vassals of Baldwin and Sibylla's cousin
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">
       Henry II of England
      </a>
      . Baldwin betrothed eight-year-old Isabella to
      <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_IV_of_Toron" title="Humphrey IV of Toron">
       Humphrey IV of Toron
      </a>
      , stepson of the powerful
      <a href="/wiki/Raynald_of_Ch%C3%A2tillon" title="Raynald of Chtillon">
       Raynald of Chtillon
      </a>
      , thereby removing her from the influence of the
      <a href="/wiki/Ibelin" title="Ibelin">
       Ibelin
      </a>
      family and that of her mother.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The dispute between the two factions in the kingdom affected the election of a new Patriarch in 1180. When Patriarch Amalric died on 6 October 1180, the two most obvious choices for his successor were William of Tyre and Heraclius of Caesarea. They were fairly evenly matched in background and education, but politically they were allied with opposite parties, as Heraclius was one of Agnes of Courtenay's supporters. The canons of the Holy Sepulchre asked the king for advice, and Heraclius was chosen through Agnes' influence. There were rumours that Agnes and Heraclius were lovers, but this information comes from the partisan 13th-century continuations of William of Tyre's history, and there is no other evidence to substantiate such a claim.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At the end of 1181, Raynald of Chtillon raided south into Arabia, in the direction of
      <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">
       Medina
      </a>
      , although he did not make it that far. It was probably around this time that Raynald also attacked a Muslim caravan. The kingdom had a truce with Saladin at the time, and Raynald's actions have been seen as an independent act of brigandage; it is possible that he was trying to prevent Saladin from moving his forces north to take control of Aleppo, which would have strengthened Saladin's position.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In response, Saladin attacked the kingdom in 1182, but was defeated at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Belvoir_Castle_(1182)" title="Battle of Belvoir Castle (1182)">
       Belvoir Castle
      </a>
      . King Baldwin, although quite ill, was still able to command the army in person. Saladin attempted to besiege Beirut from land and sea, and Baldwin raided Damascene territory, but neither side did significant damage. In December 1182,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crusader_Red_Sea_raids" title="Crusader Red Sea raids">
       Raynald launched a naval expedition
      </a>
      on the
      <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">
       Red Sea
      </a>
      , which made it as far south as
      <a href="/wiki/Rabigh" title="Rabigh">
       Rabigh
      </a>
      . The expedition was defeated and two of Raynald's men were actually taken to
      <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">
       Mecca
      </a>
      to be executed in public. Like his earlier raids, Raynald's expedition is usually seen as selfish and ultimately fatal for Jerusalem, but according to Bernard Hamilton it was actually shrewd strategy, meant to damage Saladin's prestige and reputation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1183 a general tax was levied throughout the kingdom, which was unprecedented in Jerusalem and almost all of medieval Europe to that point. The tax helped pay for larger armies for the next few years. More troops were certainly needed, since Saladin was finally able to gain control of Aleppo, and with peace in his northern territories he could focus on Jerusalem in the south. King Baldwin was so incapacitated by his leprosy that it was necessary to appoint a regent, and Guy of Lusignan was chosen, as he was Baldwin's legal heir and the king was not expected to live. The inexperienced Guy led the Frankish army against Saladin's incursions into the kingdom, but neither side made any real gains, and Guy was criticized by his opponents for not striking against Saladin when he had the chance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In October 1183 Isabella married Humphrey of Toron at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerak" title="Kerak">
       Kerak
      </a>
      , during a
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Kerak" title="Siege of Kerak">
       siege
      </a>
      by Saladin, who perhaps hoped to take some valuable prisoners. As King Baldwin, although now blind and crippled, had recovered enough to resume his reign and his command of the army, Guy was removed from the regency and his five-year-old step-son, King Baldwin's nephew
      <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_V_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin V of Jerusalem">
       and namesake Baldwin
      </a>
      , was crowned as co-king in November. King Baldwin himself then went to relieve the castle, carried on a litter, and attended by his mother. He was reconciled with Raymond of Tripoli and appointed him military commander. The siege was lifted in December and Saladin retreated to Damascus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Saladin attempted another siege in 1184, but Baldwin repelled that attack as well, and Saladin raided
      <a href="/wiki/Nablus" title="Nablus">
       Nablus
      </a>
      and other towns on the way home.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In October 1184, Guy of Lusignan led an attack on the
      <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">
       Bedouin
      </a>
      nomads from his base in Ascalon. Unlike Raynald's attacks on caravans, which may have had some military purpose, Guy attacked a group that was usually loyal to Jerusalem and provided intelligence about the movements of Saladin's troops. At the same time, King Baldwin contracted his final illness and Raymond of Tripoli, rather than Guy, was appointed as his regent. His nephew Baldwin was paraded in public, wearing his crown as Baldwin V. Baldwin IV finally succumbed to his leprosy in May 1185.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Meanwhile, the succession crisis had prompted a mission to the west to seek assistance. In 1184, Patriarch Heraclius travelled throughout the courts of Europe, but no help was forthcoming. Heraclius offered the "keys of the Holy Sepulchre, those of the Tower of David and the banner of the Kingdom of Jerusalem", but not the crown itself, to both
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">
       Philip II of France
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">
       Henry II of England
      </a>
      ; the latter, as a grandson of Fulk, was a first cousin of the royal family of Jerusalem, and had promised to go on crusade after the murder of
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Becket" title="Thomas Becket">
       Thomas Becket
      </a>
      . Both kings preferred to remain at home to defend their own territories, rather than act as regent for a child in Jerusalem. The few European knights who did travel to Jerusalem did not even see any combat, since the truce with Saladin had been re-established.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_V_of_Montferrat" title="William V of Montferrat">
       William V of Montferrat
      </a>
      was one of the few who came to his grandson Baldwin V's aid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Baldwin V's rule, with Raymond of Tripoli as regent and his great-uncle Joscelin of Edessa as his guardian, was short. He was a sickly child and died in the summer of 1186. Raymond and his supporters went to Nablus, presumably in an attempt to prevent Sibylla from claiming the throne, but Sibylla and her supporters went to Jerusalem, where it was decided that the kingdom should pass to her, on the condition that her marriage to Guy be annulled. She agreed but only if she could choose her own husband and king, and after being crowned, she immediately crowned Guy with her own hands. Raymond had refused to attend the coronation, and in Nablus he suggested that Isabella and Humphrey should be crowned instead, but Humphrey refused to agree to this plan which would have certainly started a civil war. Humphrey went to Jerusalem and swore allegiance to Guy and Sibylla, as did most of Raymond's other supporters. Raymond himself refused to do so and left for Tripoli;
      <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_of_Ibelin" title="Baldwin of Ibelin">
       Baldwin of Ibelin
      </a>
      also refused, gave up his fiefs, and left for Antioch.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_Jerusalem_and_the_Third_Crusade">
       Loss of Jerusalem and the Third Crusade
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Loss of Jerusalem and the Third Crusade">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">
       Third Crusade
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jan_Lievens-_King_Guy_of_Lusignan_and_King_Saladin.tif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="816" data-file-width="667" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Jan_Lievens-_King_Guy_of_Lusignan_and_King_Saladin.tif/lossless-page1-200px-Jan_Lievens-_King_Guy_of_Lusignan_and_King_Saladin.tif.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Jan_Lievens-_King_Guy_of_Lusignan_and_King_Saladin.tif/lossless-page1-300px-Jan_Lievens-_King_Guy_of_Lusignan_and_King_Saladin.tif.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Jan_Lievens-_King_Guy_of_Lusignan_and_King_Saladin.tif/lossless-page1-400px-Jan_Lievens-_King_Guy_of_Lusignan_and_King_Saladin.tif.png 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jan_Lievens-_King_Guy_of_Lusignan_and_King_Saladin.tif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        17th-century interpretation of (at-right)
        <a href="/wiki/Guy_of_Lusignan" title="Guy of Lusignan">
         Guy of Lusignan
        </a>
