        (born 1149 CE) being held captive by (at-left)
        <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">
         Saladin
        </a>
        (born 1135 CE), clad in a traditional (Islamic) royal garment, painted by
        <a href="/wiki/Jan_Lievens" title="Jan Lievens">
         Jan Lievens
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crusader_States_1190.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1330" data-file-width="978" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Crusader_States_1190.svg/240px-Crusader_States_1190.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Crusader_States_1190.svg/360px-Crusader_States_1190.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Crusader_States_1190.svg/480px-Crusader_States_1190.svg.png 2x" width="240"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crusader_States_1190.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Near East, c. 1190, at the outset of the Third Crusade.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Raymond of Tripoli allied with Saladin against Guy and allowed a Muslim garrison to occupy his fief in
      <a href="/wiki/Tiberias" title="Tiberias">
       Tiberias
      </a>
      , probably hoping that Saladin would help him overthrow Guy. Saladin, meanwhile, had pacified his Mesopotamian territories, and was now eager to attack the crusader kingdom; he did not intend to renew the truce when it expired in 1187. Before the truce expired, Raynald of Chatillon, the lord of Oultrejourdain and of Kerak and one of Guy's chief supporters, recognized that Saladin was massing his troops, and attacked Muslim caravans in an attempt to disrupt this. Guy was on the verge of attacking Raymond, but realized that the kingdom would need to be united in the face of the threat from Saladin, and
      <a href="/wiki/Balian_of_Ibelin" title="Balian of Ibelin">
       Balian of Ibelin
      </a>
      effected a reconciliation between the two during Easter in 1187. Saladin attacked Kerak again in April, and in May, a Muslim raiding party ran into the much smaller embassy on its way to negotiate with Raymond, and defeated it at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cresson" title="Battle of Cresson">
       Battle of Cresson
      </a>
      near Nazareth. Raymond and Guy finally agreed to attack Saladin at Tiberias, but could not agree on a plan; Raymond thought a pitched battle should be avoided, but Guy probably remembered the criticism he faced for avoiding battle in 1183, and it was decided to march out against Saladin directly. On July 4, 1187, the army of the kingdom was utterly destroyed at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hattin" title="Battle of Hattin">
       Battle of Hattin
      </a>
      . Raymond of Tripoli, Balian of Ibelin, and
      <a href="/wiki/Reginald_of_Sidon" title="Reginald of Sidon">
       Reginald of Sidon
      </a>
      escaped, but Raynald was executed by Saladin and Guy was imprisoned in Damascus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Over the next few months Saladin easily overran the entire kingdom. Only the port of Tyre remained in Frankish hands, defended by
      <a href="/wiki/Conrad_of_Montferrat" title="Conrad of Montferrat">
       Conrad of Montferrat
      </a>
      , the paternal uncle of Baldwin V, who had coincidentally arrived just in time from
      <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">
       Constantinople
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(1187)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (1187)">
       fall of Jerusalem
      </a>
      essentially ended the first Kingdom of Jerusalem. Much of the population, swollen with refugees fleeing Saladin's conquest of the surrounding territory, was allowed to flee to Tyre, Tripoli, or Egypt (whence they were sent back to Europe), but those who could not pay for their freedom were sold into slavery, and those who could were often robbed by Christians and Muslims alike on their way into exile. The capture of the city led to the
      <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">
       Third Crusade
      </a>
      , launched in 1189 and led by
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">
       Richard the Lionheart
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">
       Philip Augustus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">
       Frederick Barbarossa
      </a>
      , though the last drowned en route.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Guy of Lusignan, who had been refused entry to Tyre by Conrad, began to besiege Acre in 1189. During the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Acre_(1189%E2%80%931191)" title="Siege of Acre (11891191)">
       lengthy siege
      </a>
      , which lasted until 1191, Patriarch Heraclius, Queen Sibylla and her daughters, and many others died of disease. With the death of Sibylla in 1190, Guy now had no legal claim to the kingship, and the succession passed to Sibylla's half-sister Isabella. Isabella's mother Maria and the Ibelins (now closely allied to Conrad) argued that Isabella and Humphrey's marriage was illegal, as she had been underage at the time; underlying this was the fact that Humphrey had betrayed his wife's cause in 1186. The marriage was annulled amid some controversy. Conrad, who was now the nearest kinsman to Baldwin V in the male line, and had already proved himself a capable military leader, then married Isabella, but Guy refused to concede the crown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When Richard arrived in 1191, he and Philip took different sides in the succession dispute. Richard backed Guy, his vassal from Poitou, while Philip supported Conrad, a cousin of his late father Louis VII. After much ill-feeling and ill-health, Philip returned home in 1191, soon after the fall of Acre. Richard defeated Saladin at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Arsuf" title="Battle of Arsuf">
       Battle of Arsuf
      </a>
      in 1191 and the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jaffa_(1192)" title="Battle of Jaffa (1192)">
       Battle of Jaffa
      </a>
      in 1192, recovering most of the coast, but could not recover Jerusalem or any of the inland territory of the kingdom. It has been suggested that this may have actually been a strategic decision by Richard rather than a failure as such, as he may have recognized that Jerusalem in particular was in fact a strategic liability as long as the crusaders were obligated to defend it, as it was isolated from the sea where Western reinforcements could arrive.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Conrad was unanimously elected king in April 1192, but was murdered by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hashshashin" title="Hashshashin">
       Hashshashin
      </a>
      only days later. Eight days after that, the pregnant Isabella was married to Count
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_Champagne" title="Henry II of Champagne">
       Henry II of Champagne
      </a>
      , nephew of Richard and Philip, but politically allied to Richard. As compensation, Richard sold Guy the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Cyprus" title="Kingdom of Cyprus">
       island of Cyprus
      </a>
      , which Richard had captured on the way to Acre, although Guy continued to claim the throne of Jerusalem until his death in 1194.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The crusade came to an end peacefully, with the
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ramla" title="Treaty of Ramla">
       Treaty of Ramla
      </a>
      negotiated in 1192; Saladin allowed pilgrimages to be made to Jerusalem, allowing the crusaders to fulfill their vows, after which they all returned home. The native crusader barons set about rebuilding their kingdom from Acre and the other coastal cities.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Kingdom_of_Acre">
       The Kingdom of Acre
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The Kingdom of Acre">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      For the next hundred years, the Kingdom of Jerusalem clung to life as a tiny kingdom hugging the Syrian coastline. Its capital was moved to Acre and controlled most of the coastline of present day Israel and southern and central Lebanon, including the strongholds and towns of Jaffa, Arsuf, Caesarea, Tyre, Sidon, and Beirut. At best, it included only a few other significant cities, such as Ascalon and some interior fortresses, as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">
       suzerainty
      </a>
      over Tripoli and Antioch. The new king, Henry of Champagne, died accidentally in 1197, and Isabella married for a fourth time, to
      <a href="/wiki/Aimery_of_Jerusalem" title="Aimery of Jerusalem">
       Aimery of Lusignan
      </a>
      , Guy's brother. Aimery had already inherited Cyprus from Guy, and had been crowned king by Frederick Barbarossa's son, Emperor
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry VI, Holy Roman Emperor">
       Henry VI
      </a>
      . Henry led a
      <a href="/wiki/Crusade_of_1197" title="Crusade of 1197">
       crusade in 1197
      </a>
      but died along the way. Nevertheless, his troops recaptured Beirut and Sidon for the kingdom before returning home in 1198.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A five-year truce was then concluded with the Ayyubids in Syria in 1198.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Ayyubid empire had fallen into civil war after the death of Saladin in 1193. His sons claimed various parts of his empire:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malik_az-Zahir" title="Malik az-Zahir">
       az-Zahir
      </a>
      took control of Aleppo,
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Aziz_Uthman" title="Al-Aziz Uthman">
       al-Aziz Uthman
      </a>
      held Cairo, while his eldest son,
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Afdal_ibn_Salah_ad-Din" title="Al-Afdal ibn Salah ad-Din">
       al-Afdal
      </a>
      , retained Damascus. Saladin's brother
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Adil_I" title="Al-Adil I">
       Al-Adil Sayf ad-Din
      </a>
      (often called "Saphadin" by the crusaders) acquired
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Jazira,_Mesopotamia" title="Al-Jazira, Mesopotamia">
       al-Jazira
      </a>
      (northern Mesopotamia), and al-Adil's son
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27azzam" title="Al-Mu'azzam">
       al-Mu'azzam
      </a>
      took possession of
      <a href="/wiki/Al_Karak" title="Al Karak">
       Karak
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Oultrejordain" title="Oultrejordain">
       Transjordan
      </a>
      . In 1196, al-Afdal was driven out of Damascus by Uthman. He returned when Uthman died in 1198, but was opposed by al-Adil, who occupied the
      <a href="/wiki/Citadel_of_Damascus" title="Citadel of Damascus">
       Citadel of Damascus
      </a>
      . Al-Adil also conquered Cairo in 1200 and banished al-Afdal from Damascus in 1201. He proclaimed himself Sultan of Egypt and Syria, entrusting Damascus to al-Mu'azzam and al-Jazira to his other son
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Kamil" title="Al-Kamil">
       al-Kamil
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Meanwhile, schemes were hatched to reconquer Jerusalem through Egypt. A
      <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">
       Fourth Crusade
      </a>
      was planned after the failure of the Third, but it resulted in the
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(1204)" title="Siege of Constantinople (1204)">
       sack of Constantinople
      </a>
      in 1204, and most of the crusaders involved never arrived in the kingdom. Aimery, however, not knowing of the diversion to Constantinople, raided Egypt in advance of the expected invasion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Both Isabella and Aimery died in 1205 and again an underage girl, Isabella and Conrad's daughter
      <a href="/wiki/Maria_of_Montferrat" title="Maria of Montferrat">
       Maria of Montferrat
      </a>
      , became queen of Jerusalem. Isabella's half-brother
      <a href="/wiki/John_of_Ibelin,_the_Old_Lord_of_Beirut" title="John of Ibelin, the Old Lord of Beirut">
       John of Ibelin, the Old Lord of Beirut
      </a>
      governed as regent until 1210 when Maria married an experienced French knight,
      <a href="/wiki/John_of_Brienne" title="John of Brienne">
       John of Brienne
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Maria died in childbirth in 1212, and John of Brienne continued to rule as regent for their daughter
      <a href="/wiki/Isabella_II_of_Jerusalem" title="Isabella II of Jerusalem">
       Isabella II
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fifth_Crusade_and_Frederick_II">
       Fifth Crusade and Frederick II
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fifth Crusade and Frederick II">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:193px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al-Kamil_Muhammad_al-Malik_and_Frederick_II_Holy_Roman_Emperor.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="191" height="393" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Al-Kamil_Muhammad_al-Malik_and_Frederick_II_Holy_Roman_Emperor.jpg" width="191"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al-Kamil_Muhammad_al-Malik_and_Frederick_II_Holy_Roman_Emperor.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Frederick II (left) meets al-Kamil (right).
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Nuova_Cronica" title="Nuova Cronica">
          Nuova Cronica
         </a>
        </i>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Villani" title="Giovanni Villani">
         Giovanni Villani
        </a>
        (14th century).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_Lateran_Council" title="Fourth Lateran Council">
       Fourth Lateran Council
      </a>
      in 1215 called for a new, better-organized crusade against Egypt. In late 1217
      <a href="/wiki/Andrew_II_of_Hungary" title="Andrew II of Hungary">
       Andrew II of Hungary
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Leopold_VI,_Duke_of_Austria" title="Leopold VI, Duke of Austria">
       Leopold VI, Duke of Austria
      </a>
      arrived in Acre and, along with John of Brienne, raided territory further inland, including
      <a href="/wiki/Mount_Tabor" title="Mount Tabor">
       Mount Tabor
      </a>
      , but without success.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the departure of the Hungarians, the remaining crusaders set about refortifying
      <a href="/wiki/Caesarea_Maritima" title="Caesarea Maritima">
       Caesarea
      </a>
      and the Templar fortress of
      <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_P%C3%A8lerin" title="Chteau Plerin">
       Chteau Plerin
      </a>
      throughout the winter of 1217 and spring of 1218.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the spring of 1218 the
      <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Crusade" title="Fifth Crusade">
       Fifth Crusade
      </a>
      began in earnest when German crusader fleets landed at Acre. Along with King John, who was elected leader of the crusade, the fleets sailed to Egypt and besieged
      <a href="/wiki/Damietta" title="Damietta">
       Damietta
      </a>
      at the mouth of the Nile in May. The siege progressed slowly, and the Egyptian sultan al-Adil died in August 1218, supposedly of shock after the crusaders managed to capture one of Damietta's towers. He was succeeded by his son
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Kamil" title="Al-Kamil">
       al-Kamil
      </a>
      . In the autumn of 1218 reinforcements arrived from Europe, including the papal legate
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelagius_of_Albano" title="Pelagius of Albano">
       Pelagius of Albano
      </a>
      . In the winter the crusaders were affected by floods and disease, and the siege dragged on throughout 1219, when
      <a href="/wiki/Francis_of_Assisi" title="Francis of Assisi">
       Francis of Assisi
      </a>
      arrived to attempt to negotiate a truce. Neither side could agree to terms, despite the Ayyubid offer of a thirty-year truce and the restoration of Jerusalem and most of the rest of the former kingdom. The crusaders finally managed to starve out the city and captured it in November. Al-Kamil retreated to the nearby fortress of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Mansurah" title="Al-Mansurah">
       al-Mansurah
      </a>
      , but the crusaders remained in Damietta throughout 1219 and 1220, awaiting the arrival of Holy Roman Emperor
      <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">
       Frederick II
      </a>
      , while King John returned to Acre briefly to defend against al-Mu'azzam, who was raiding the kingdom from Damascus in John's absence. Still expecting the emperor's imminent arrival, in July 1221, the crusaders set off towards Cairo, but they were stopped by the
      <a href="/wiki/Flooding_of_the_Nile" title="Flooding of the Nile">
       rising Nile
      </a>
      , which al-Kamil allowed to flood by breaking the dams along its course. The sultan easily defeated the trapped crusader army and regained Damietta. Emperor Frederick had, in fact, never left Europe at all.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After the failure of the crusade, John travelled throughout Europe seeking assistance, but found support only from Frederick, who then married John and Maria's daughter Isabella II in 1225. The next year, Isabella died giving birth to their son
      <a href="/wiki/Conrad_IV_of_Germany" title="Conrad IV of Germany">
       Conrad IV
      </a>
      , who succeeded his mother to the throne although he never appeared in the east. Frederick had reneged on his promise to lead the Fifth Crusade, but was now eager to cement his claim to the throne through Conrad. There were also plans to join with al-Kamil in attacking al-Mu'azzam in Damascus, an alliance which had been discussed with Egyptian envoys in Italy. But after continually delaying his departure for the Holy Land, including suffering an outbreak of disease in his fleet, he was excommunicated by
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_IX" title="Pope Gregory IX">
       Pope Gregory IX
      </a>
      in 1227. The crusaders, led not by Frederick but by his representatives
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Filangieri" title="Richard Filangieri">
       Richard Filangieri
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Duke_of_Limburg" title="Henry IV, Duke of Limburg">
       Henry IV, Duke of Limburg
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermann_of_Salza" title="Hermann of Salza">
       Hermann of Salza
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Master_of_the_Teutonic_Knights" title="Grand Master of the Teutonic Knights">
       Grand Master of the Teutonic Knights
      </a>
      , arrived in the east late in 1227, and while waiting for the emperor they set about refortifying
      <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">
       Sidon
      </a>
      , where they built the
      <a href="/wiki/Sidon_Sea_Castle" title="Sidon Sea Castle">
       sea castle
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Montfort_Castle" title="Montfort Castle">
       Montfort
      </a>
      , which later became the headquarters of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" title="Teutonic Knights">
       Teutonic Knights
      </a>
      . The Ayyubids of Damascus did not dare attack, as al-Mu'azzam had suddenly died not long before. Frederick finally arrived on the
      <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Crusade" title="Sixth Crusade">
       Sixth Crusade
      </a>
      in September 1228, and claimed the regency of the kingdom in the name of his infant son.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Frederick immediately came into conflict with the native nobles of Outremer, some of whom resented his attempts to impose Imperial authority over both Cyprus and Jerusalem. The Cypriot nobles were already quarrelling amongst themselves about the regency for
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_Cyprus" title="Henry I of Cyprus">
       Henry I of Cyprus
      </a>
      , who was still a child. The High Court of Cyprus had elected John of Ibelin as regent, but Henry's mother
      <a href="/wiki/Alice_of_Champagne" title="Alice of Champagne">
       Alice of Champagne
      </a>
      wished to appoint one of her supporters; Alice and her party, members or supporters of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lusignan_dynasty" title="Lusignan dynasty">
       Lusignan dynasty
      </a>
      , sided with Frederick, whose father had crowned Aimery of Lusignan king in 1197. At
      <a href="/wiki/Limassol" title="Limassol">
       Limassol
      </a>
      , Frederick demanded that John give up not only the regency of Cyprus, but also John's own lordship of Beirut on the mainland. John argued that Frederick had no legal authority to make such demands and refused to give up either title. Frederick then imprisoned John's sons as hostages to guarantee John's support for his crusade.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edbury.2C_pp._57-64_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edbury.2C_pp._57-64-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      John did accompany Frederick to the mainland, but Frederick was not well-received there; one of his few supporters was
      <a href="/wiki/Balian_Grenier" title="Balian Grenier">
       Balian, Lord of Sidon
      </a>
      , who had welcomed the crusaders the year before and now acted as an ambassador to the Ayyubids. The death of al-Mu'azzam negated the proposed alliance with al-Kamil, who along with his brother
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Ashraf" title="Al-Ashraf">
       al-Ashraf
      </a>
      had taken possession of Damascus (as well as Jerusalem) from their nephew, al-Mu'azzam's son
      <a href="/wiki/An-Nasir_Dawud" title="An-Nasir Dawud">
       an-Nasir Dawud
      </a>
      . However, al-Kamil presumably did not know of the small size of Frederick's army, nor the divisions within it caused by his excommunication, and wished to avoid defending his territories against another crusade. Frederick's presence alone was sufficient to regain Jerusalem, Bethlehem, Nazareth, and a number of surrounding castles without a fight: these were recovered in February 1229, in return for a ten-year truce with the Ayyubids and freedom of worship for Jerusalem's Muslim inhabitants. The terms of the treaty were unacceptable to the Patriarch of Jerusalem
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gerald_of_Lausanne&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gerald of Lausanne (page does not exist)">
       Gerald of Lausanne
      </a>
      , who placed the city under
      <a href="/wiki/Interdict" title="Interdict">
       interdict
      </a>
      . In March, Frederick crowned himself in the Church of the Holy Sepulchre, but because of his excommunication and the interdict Jerusalem was never truly reincorporated into the kingdom, which continued to be ruled from Acre.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Meanwhile, in Italy, the Pope had used Frederick's excommunication as an excuse to invade his Italian territories; the papal armies were led by Frederick's former father-in-law John of Brienne. Frederick was forced to return home in 1229, leaving the Holy Land "not in triumph, but showered with offal" by the citizens of Acre.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_the_Lombards_and_the_Barons.27_Crusade">
       War of the Lombards and the Barons' Crusade
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: War of the Lombards and the Barons' Crusade">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JanBrienne.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="400" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/JanBrienne.jpg/220px-JanBrienne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/JanBrienne.jpg/330px-JanBrienne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5a/JanBrienne.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JanBrienne.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Coronation of
        <a href="/wiki/Maria_of_Montferrat" title="Maria of Montferrat">
         Maria of Montferrat
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/John_of_Brienne" title="John of Brienne">
         John of Brienne
        </a>
        , King of Jerusalem and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Emperor_of_Constantinople" title="Latin Emperor of Constantinople">
         Latin Emperor of Constantinople
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Nevertheless, Frederick
      <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Lombards" title="War of the Lombards">
       sent an Imperial army
      </a>
      in 1231, under Richard Filangieri, who occupied Beirut and Tyre, but was unable to gain control of Acre. John's supporters formed a
      <a href="/wiki/Medieval_commune" title="Medieval commune">
       commune
      </a>
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Commune_of_Acre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Commune of Acre (page does not exist)">
       in Acre
      </a>
      , of which John himself was elected mayor in 1232. With the help of the
      <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">
       Genoese
      </a>
      merchants, the commune recaptured Beirut. John also attacked Tyre, but was defeated by Filangieri at the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Casal_Imbert&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Battle of Casal Imbert (page does not exist)">
       Battle of Casal Imbert
      </a>
      in May 1232.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edbury.2C_pp._57-64_72-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edbury.2C_pp._57-64-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On Cyprus, King Henry I came of age in 1232 and John's regency was no longer necessary. Both John and Filangieri raced back to Cyprus to assert their authority, and the imperial forces were defeated at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Agridi" title="Battle of Agridi">
       Battle of Agridi
      </a>
      on June 15. Henry became undisputed king of Cyprus, but continued to support the Ibelins over the Lusignans and the imperial party. On the mainland, Filangieri had the support of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bohemund_IV_of_Antioch" title="Bohemund IV of Antioch">
       Bohemund IV of Antioch
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" title="Teutonic Knights">
       Teutonic Knights
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Knights_Hospitaller" title="Knights Hospitaller">
       Knights Hospitaller
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">
       Pisan
      </a>
      merchants. John was supported by his nobles on Cyprus, and by his continental holdings in Beirut,
      <a href="/wiki/Caesarea" title="Caesarea">
       Caesarea
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Arsuf" title="Arsuf">
       Arsuf
      </a>
      , as well as by the
      <a href="/wiki/Knights_Templar" title="Knights Templar">
       Knights Templar
      </a>
      and the Genoese. Neither side could make any headway, and in 1234 Gregory IX excommunicated John and his supporters. This was partly revoked in 1235, but still no peace could be made. John died in 1236 and the war was taken up by his son
      <a href="/wiki/Balian_of_Beirut" title="Balian of Beirut">
       Balian of Beirut
      </a>
      and his nephew
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Montfort,_Lord_of_Tyre" title="Philip of Montfort, Lord of Tyre">
       Philip of Montfort
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyerman.2C_pp._725-726_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tyerman.2C_pp._725-726-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Meanwhile, the treaty with the Ayyubids was set to expire in 1239. Plans for a new crusade to be led by Frederick came to nothing, and Frederick himself was excommunicated by Gregory IX again in 1239. However, other European nobles took up the cause, including
      <a href="/wiki/Theobald_I_of_Navarre" title="Theobald I of Navarre">
       Theobald IV
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Count_of_Champagne" title="Count of Champagne">
       Count of Champagne
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Navarre" title="King of Navarre">
       King of Navarre
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Peter_I,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="Peter I, Duke of Brittany">
       Peter of Dreux
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Amaury_VI_of_Montfort" title="Amaury VI of Montfort">
       Amaury VI of Montfort
      </a>
      , who arrived in Acre in September 1239. Theobald was elected leader of the crusade at a council in Acre, attended by the most of the important nobles of the kingdom, including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walter_IV_of_Brienne" title="Walter IV of Brienne">
       Walter of Brienne
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/John_of_Arsuf" title="John of Arsuf">
       John of Arsuf
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Balian_Grenier" title="Balian Grenier">
       Balian of Sidon
      </a>
      . The arrival of the crusade was a brief respite from the Lombard War; Filangieri remained in Tyre and did not participate. The council decided to refortify Ascalon in the south and attack Damascus in the north.
     </p>
     <p>
      The crusaders may have been aware of the new divisions among the Ayyubids; al-Kamil had occupied Damascus in 1238 but had died soon afterwards, and his territory was inherited by his family. His sons
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Adil_II" title="Al-Adil II">
       al-Adil abu Bakr
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/As-Salih_Ayyub" title="As-Salih Ayyub">
       as-Salih Ayyub
      </a>
      inherited Egypt and Damascus. Ayyub marched on Cairo in an attempt to drive out al-Adil, but during his absence al-Kamil's brother
      <a href="/wiki/As-Salih_Ismail" title="As-Salih Ismail">
       as-Salih Isma'il
      </a>
      took over Damascus, and Ayyub was taken prisoner by an-Nasir Dawud. The crusaders, meanwhile, marched to Ascalon. Along the way, Walter of Brienne captured livestock intended to resupply Damascus, as the Ayyubids had probably learned of the crusaders' plans to attack it. The victory was short-lived, however, as the crusaders were then defeated by the Egyptian army at Gaza in November of 1239.
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_II,_Count_of_Bar" title="Henry II, Count of Bar">
       Henry II, Count of Bar
      </a>
      was killed and Amaury of Montfort captured. The crusaders returned to Acre, possibly because the native barons of the kingdom were suspicious of Filangieri in Tyre. Dawud took advantage of the Ayyubid victory to recapture Jerusalem in December, the ten-year truce having expired.
     </p>
     <p>
      Although Ayyub was Dawud's prisoner, the two now allied against al-Adil in Egypt, which Ayyub seized in 1240. In Damascus, Isma'il recognized the threat of Dawud and Ayyub against his own possessions, and turned to the crusaders for assistance. Theobald concluded a treaty with Isma'il, in return for territorial concessions that restored Jerusalem to Christian control, as well as much of the rest of the former kingdom, even more territory than Frederick had recovered in 1229. Theobald, however, was frustrated by the Lombard War, and returned home in September 1240. Almost immediately after Theobald's departure,
      <a href="/wiki/Richard,_1st_Earl_of_Cornwall" title="Richard, 1st Earl of Cornwall">
       Richard of Cornwall
      </a>
      arrived. He completed the rebuilding of Ascalon, and also made peace with Ayyub in Egypt. Ayyub confirmed Isma'il's concessions in 1241, and prisoners taken at Gaza were exchanged by both sides. Richard returned to Europe in 1241.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although the kingdom had essentially been restored, the Lombard War continued to occupy the kingdom's nobility. As the Templars and Hospitallers supported opposite sides, they also attacked each other, and the Templars broke the treaty with the Ayyubids by attacking Nablus in 1241. Conrad proclaimed that he had come of age in 1242, eliminating both Frederick's claim to the regency and the need for an imperial guardian to govern in his place, although he had not yet turned 15, the age of majority according to the customs of Jerusalem. Through Conrad, Frederick tried to send an imperial regent, but the anti-imperial faction in Acre argued that Jerusalem's laws allowed them to appoint their own regent. In June the
      <i>
       Haute Cour
      </i>
      granted the regency to Alice of Champagne, who, as the daughter of Isabella I, was Conrad's great-aunt and his closest relative living in the kingdom. Alice ordered Filangieri to be arrested, and along with the Ibelins and Venetians, besieged Tyre, which fell in July 1243. The Lombard War was over, but the king was still absent, as Conrad never came to the east. Alice was prevented from exercising any real power as regent by Philip of Montfort, who took control of Tyre, and Balian of Beirut, who continued to hold Acre.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyerman.2C_pp._725-726_75-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tyerman.2C_pp._725-726-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Crusade_of_Louis_IX">
       Crusade of Louis IX
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Crusade of Louis IX">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Ayyubids were still divided between Ayyub in Egypt, Isma'il in Damascus, and Dawud in Kerak. Isma'il, Dawud, and
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Mansur_Ibrahim" title="Al-Mansur Ibrahim">
       al-Mansur Ibrahim
      </a>
      of Homs went to war with Ayyub, who hired the
      <a href="/wiki/Khwarazmian_dynasty" title="Khwarazmian dynasty">
       Khwarazmians
      </a>
      to fight for him. The Khwarazmians were nomadic Turks from central Asia, who had recently been displaced by the
      <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">
       Mongols
      </a>
      further to the east and were now residing in Mesopotamia. With Ayyub's support they
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(1244)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (1244)">
       sacked Jerusalem
      </a>
      in the summer of 1244, leaving it in ruins and useless to both Christians and Muslims. In October, the Khwarazmians, along with the Egyptian army under the command of
      <a href="/wiki/Baibars" title="Baibars">
       Baibars
      </a>
      , were met by the Frankish army, led by Philip of Montfort, Walter of Brienne, and the masters of the Templars, Hospitallers, and Teutonic Knights, along with al-Mansur and Dawud. On October 17 the Egyptian-Khwarazmian army destroyed the Frankish-Syrian coalition, and Walter of Brienne was taken captive and later executed. By 1247, Ayyub had reoccupied most of the territory that had been conceded in 1239, and had also gained control of Damascus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Crusade" title="Seventh Crusade">
       A new crusade
      </a>
      was discussed at the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Lyon" title="First Council of Lyon">
       Council of Lyon
      </a>
      in 1245 by
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">
       Pope Innocent IV
      </a>
      . The council deposed Frederick II, so no help could be expected from the empire, but King
      <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX_of_France" title="Louis IX of France">
       Louis IX of France
      </a>
      had already vowed to go on crusade. Louis arrived in Cyprus in 1248, where he gathered an army of his own men, including his brothers
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_of_Artois" title="Robert of Artois">
       Robert of Artois
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_of_Anjou" title="Charles of Anjou">
       Charles of Anjou
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alphonse_of_Poitiers" title="Alphonse of Poitiers">
       Alphonse of Poitiers
      </a>
      , and those of Cyprus and Jerusalem, led by the Ibelin family
      <a href="/wiki/John_of_Ibelin_(jurist)" title="John of Ibelin (jurist)">
       John of Jaffa
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Guy_of_Ibelin,_constable_of_Cyprus" title="Guy of Ibelin, constable of Cyprus">
       Guy of Ibelin
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Balian_of_Beirut" title="Balian of Beirut">
       Balian of Beirut
      </a>
      . Once again the target was Egypt. Damietta was captured without resistance when the crusaders landed in June 1249, but the crusade halted there until November, by which time the Egyptian sultan Ayyub had died and had been succeeded by his son
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turanshah" title="Turanshah">
       Turanshah
      </a>
      . In February, the crusaders were defeated at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_al-Mansurah" title="Battle of al-Mansurah">
       Battle of al-Mansurah
      </a>
      , where Robert of Artois was killed. The crusaders were unable to cross the Nile, and, suffering from disease and lack of supplies, retreated towards Damietta in April. They were defeated along the way at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fariskur" title="Battle of Fariskur">
       Battle of Fariskur
      </a>
      , with Louis being taken captive by Turanshah. During Louis' captivity, Turanshah was overthrown by his Mamluk soldiers, led by the general
      <a href="/wiki/Aybak" title="Aybak">
       Aybak
      </a>
      , who then released Louis in May in return for Damietta and a large ransom. For the next four years Louis resided in Acre, and helped refortify that city along with Caesarea, Jaffa, and Sidon. He also made truces with the Ayyubids in Syria, and sent embassies to negotiate with the Mongols, who were beginning to threaten the Muslim world. before returning home in 1254. He left behind a large garrison of French soldiers in Acre, under the command of
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Geoffrey_of_Sergines&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Geoffrey of Sergines (page does not exist)">
       Geoffrey of Sergines
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the midst of these events, Alice of Champagne had died in 1246 and had been replaced as regent by her son King
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_Cyprus" title="Henry I of Cyprus">
       Henry I of Cyprus
      </a>
      , for whom John of Jaffa served as
      <i>
       bailli
      </i>
      in Acre. During Louis IX's stay in Acre, Henry I died in 1253, and was succeeded in Cyprus by his infant son
      <a href="/wiki/Hugh_II_of_Cyprus" title="Hugh II of Cyprus">
       Hugh II
      </a>
      . Hugh was technically regent of Jerusalem as well, both for Conrad and for Conrad's son
      <a href="/wiki/Conradin" title="Conradin">
       Conradin
      </a>
      after Conrad died in 1254. Both Cyprus and Jerusalem were governed by Hugh's mother
      <a href="/wiki/Plaisance_of_Antioch" title="Plaisance of Antioch">
       Plaisance of Antioch
      </a>
      , but John remained
      <i>
       bailli
      </i>
      for Hugh in Acre. John made peace with Damascus and attempted to regain Ascalon; the Egyptians, now ruled by the
      <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">
       Mamluk sultanate
      </a>
      , besieged Jaffa in 1256 in response. John defeated them, and afterwards gave up the bailliage to his cousin
      <a href="/wiki/John_of_Arsuf" title="John of Arsuf">
       John of Arsuf
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_Saint_Sabas">
       War of Saint Sabas
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: War of Saint Sabas">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1256 the commercial rivalry between the Venetian and Genoese merchant colonies
      <a href="/wiki/War_of_Saint_Sabas" title="War of Saint Sabas">
       broke out into open warfare
      </a>
      . In Acre, the two colonies disputed possession of the monastery of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Sabas" title="Saint Sabas">
       Saint Sabas
      </a>
      . The Genoese, assisted by the Pisan merchants, attacked the Venetian quarter and burned their ships, but the Venetians drove them out. The Venetians were then expelled from Tyre by Philip of Monfort. John of Arsuf, John of Jaffa,
      <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Beirut" title="John II of Beirut">
       John II of Beirut
      </a>
      , the Templars, and the Teutonic Knights supported the Venetians, who also convinced the Pisans to join them, while the Hospitallers supported the Genoese. In 1257 the Venetians conquered the monastery and destroyed its fortifications, although they were unable to expel the Genoese completely. They blockaded the Genoese quarter, but the Genoese were supplied by the Hospitallers, whose complex was nearby, and by Philip of Montfort who sent food from Tyre. In August 1257, John of Arsuf tried to end the war by granting commercial rights in Acre to Ancona, an Italian ally of Genoa, but aside from Philip of Montfort and the Hospitallers, the rest of the nobles continued to support Venice. In June of 1258, Philip and the Hospitallers marched on Acre while a Genoese fleet attacked the city by sea. The naval battle was won by Venice, and the Genoese were forced to abandon their quarter and flee to Tyre with Philip. The war also spread to Tripoli and Antioch, where the
      <a href="/wiki/Embriaco_family" title="Embriaco family">
       Embriaco family
      </a>
      , descended from Genoese crusaders, were pitted against
      <a href="/wiki/Bohemond_VI_of_Antioch" title="Bohemond VI of Antioch">
       Bohemond VI of Antioch
      </a>
      , who supported the Venetians. In 1261 the Patriarch,
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_IV" title="Pope Urban IV">
       Jacques Pantaleon
      </a>
      , organised a council to re-establish order in the kingdom, though the Genoese did not return to Acre.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mongols">
       Mongols
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mongols">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      It was during this period that the Mongols arrived in the Near East. Their presence further east had already displaced the Khwarazmians, and embassies had been sent by various popes as well as Louis IX to ally or negotiate with them, but they were
      <a href="/wiki/Franco-Mongol_alliance" title="Franco-Mongol alliance">
       uninterested in alliances
      </a>
      . They
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">
       sacked Baghdad
      </a>
      in 1258,
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Aleppo_(1260)" title="Siege of Aleppo (1260)">
       and Aleppo
      </a>
      and Damascus in 1260, destroying both the Abbasid caliphate and the last vestiges of the Ayyubid dynasty.
      <a href="/wiki/Hethum_I,_King_of_Armenia" title="Hethum I, King of Armenia">
       Hethum I of Armenia
      </a>
      and Bohemond VI of Antioch had already submitted to the Mongols as vassals. Some of the Mongols were Nestorian Christians, including
      <a href="/wiki/Kitbuqa" title="Kitbuqa">
       Kitbuqa
      </a>
      , one of the generals at the sieges of Baghdad and Damascus, but despite this, the nobles of Acre refused to submit. As the kingdom was by now a relatively unimportant state, the Mongols paid little attention to it, but there were a few skirmishes in 1260: the forces of
      <a href="/wiki/Julian_Grenier" title="Julian Grenier">
       Julian of Sidon
      </a>
      killed the nephew of Kitbuqa, who responded by sacking Sidon, and John II of Beirut was also captured by the Mongols during another raid. The apparently inevitable Mongol conquest was stalled when
      <a href="/wiki/Hulagu_Khan" title="Hulagu Khan">
       Hulagu
      </a>
      , the Mongol commander in Syria, returned home after the death of his brother
      <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Mngke Khan">
       Mngke Khan
      </a>
      , leaving Kitbuqa with a small garrison. The Mamluks of Egypt then sought, and were granted, permission to advance through Frankish territory, and defeated the Mongols at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ain_Jalut" title="Battle of Ain Jalut">
       Battle of Ain Jalut
      </a>
      in September 1260. Kitbuqa was killed and all of Syria fell under Mamluk control. On the way back to Egypt, the Mamluk sultan
      <a href="/wiki/Qutuz" title="Qutuz">
       Qutuz
      </a>
      was assassinated by the general Baibars, who was far less favourable than his predecessor to alliances with the Franks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_of_Acre">
       Fall of Acre
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fall of Acre">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      John of Arsuf had died in 1258 and was replaced as
      <i>
       bailli
      </i>
      by Geoffrey of Sergines, Louis IX's lieutenant in Acre. Plaisance died in 1261, but as her son Hugh II was still underage, Cyprus passed to his cousin
      <a href="/wiki/Hugh_III_of_Cyprus" title="Hugh III of Cyprus">
       Hugh of Antioch-Lusignan
      </a>
      , whose mother
      <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_Cyprus" title="Isabella of Cyprus">
       Isabella of Cyprus
      </a>
      , Alice of Champagne and Hugh I of Cyprus' daughter and Hugh II's aunt, took over the regency in Acre. She appointed as
      <i>
       bailli
      </i>
      her husband
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Antioch" title="Henry of Antioch">
       Henry of Antioch
      </a>
      (who was also Plaisance's uncle), but died in 1264. The regency in Acre was then claimed by Hugh of Antioch-Lusignan and his cousin
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugh_of_Brienne" title="Hugh of Brienne">
       Hugh of Brienne
      </a>
      , and Hugh II died in 1267 before he reached the age of majority. Hugh of Antioch-Lusignan won the dispute and succeeded Hugh II on Cyprus as Hugh III. When Conradin was executed in Sicily in 1268, there was no other Hohenstaufen heir to succeed him, and Hugh III inherited the Kingdom of Jerusalem as well in 1269. This was disputed by another branch of the Lusignan family:
      <a href="/wiki/Maria_of_Antioch" title="Maria of Antioch">
       Maria of Antioch
      </a>
      , daughter of Bohemond IV of Antioch and
      <a href="/wiki/Melisende_of_Lusignan" title="Melisende of Lusignan">
       Melisende of Lusignan
      </a>
      (herself a daughter of Isabella I and Amalric II), claimed the throne as the oldest living relative of Isabella I, but for the moment her claim was ignored. By this time, the Mamluks under Baibars were taking advantage of the kingdom's constant disputes, and began conquering the remaining crusader cities along the coast. In 1265, Baibars took Caesarea, Haifa and Arsuf, and Safad and Toron in 1266. In 1268 he captured Jaffa and Beaufort, and then
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Antioch_(1268)" title="Siege of Antioch (1268)">
       besieged and destroyed Antioch
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KrakDesChevaliers.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/KrakDesChevaliers.jpg/220px-KrakDesChevaliers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/KrakDesChevaliers.jpg/330px-KrakDesChevaliers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/KrakDesChevaliers.jpg/440px-KrakDesChevaliers.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KrakDesChevaliers.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Krak_des_Chevaliers" title="Krak des Chevaliers">
         Krak des Chevaliers
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
         Syria
        </a>
        .
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">
         UNESCO World Heritage Site
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Hugh III and Baibars made a one-year truce after these conquests; Baibars knew that Louis IX was planning another crusade from Europe, and assumed that the target would once again be Egypt. But instead the crusade was diverted to
      <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">
       Tunis
      </a>
      , where Louis died. Baibars was free to continue his campaigns: in 1270 he had the Assassins kill Philip of Montfort, and in 1271 he captured the Hospitaller and Teutonic Knights strongholds of Krak des Chevaliers and Montfort Castle. He also
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tripoli_(1271)" title="Siege of Tripoli (1271)">
       besieged Tripoli
      </a>
      , but abandoned it in May when
      <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">
       Prince Edward of England
      </a>
      arrived, the only part of Louis IX's crusade to arrive in the east. Edward could do nothing except arrange a ten-year truce with Baibars, who nevertheless attempted to have him assassinated as well. Edward left in 1272, and despite the
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">
       Second Council of Lyon
      </a>
      's plans for another crusade in 1274, no further large-scale expedition ever arrived. Hugh III's authority on the mainland began to break down; he was an unpopular king, and Beirut, the only territory left outside of Acre and Tyre, started to act independently. Its heiress,
      <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_Ibelin,_Queen_of_Cyprus" title="Isabella of Ibelin, Queen of Cyprus">
       Isabella of Ibelin
      </a>
      (widow of Hugh II), actually placed it under Baibars' protection. Finding the mainland ungovernable, Hugh III left for Cyprus, leaving
      <a href="/wiki/Balian_of_Arsuf" title="Balian of Arsuf">
       Balian of Arsuf
      </a>
      as
      <i>
       bailli
      </i>
      . Then in 1277, Maria of Antioch sold her claim to the kingdom to Charles of Anjou, who sent
      <a href="/wiki/Roger_of_San_Severino" title="Roger of San Severino">
       Roger of San Severino
      </a>
      to represent him. The Venetians and Templars supported the claim, and Balian was powerless to oppose him. Baibars died in 1277 and was succeeded by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qalawun" title="Qalawun">
       Qalawun
      </a>
      . In 1281 the ten-year truce expired and was renewed by Roger. Roger returned to Europe after the
      <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_Vespers" title="Sicilian Vespers">
       Sicilian Vespers
      </a>
      in 1282, and was replaced by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Odo_Poilechien&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Odo Poilechien (page does not exist)">
       Odo Poilechien
      </a>
      . Hugh III attempted to re-assert his authority on the mainland by landing at Beirut in 1283, but this was ineffective and he died in Tyre in 1284. He was succeeded briefly by his son
      <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Jerusalem" title="John II of Jerusalem">
       John II
      </a>
      , who died soon after in 1285, and was succeeded by his brother, Hugh III's other son
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_Jerusalem" title="Henry II of Jerusalem">
       Henry II
      </a>
      . That year Qalawun captured the Hospitaller fortress of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marqab" title="Marqab">
       Marqab
      </a>
      . Charles of Anjou also died in 1285, and the military orders and the commune of Acre accepted Henry II as king; Odo Poilechen refused to recognize him, but was allowed to hand Acre over to the Templars rather than Henry directly, and the Templars then handed it to the king. War broke out between the Venetians and Genoese again in 1287, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tripoli_(1289)" title="Siege of Tripoli (1289)">
       Tripoli fell
      </a>
      to Qalawun in 1289. Although it was only a matter of time before Acre also fell, the end of the crusader kingdom was actually instigated in 1290 by newly arrived crusaders, who rioted in Acre and attacked the city's Muslim merchants. Qalawun died before he could retaliate, but his son
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Ashraf_Khalil" title="Al-Ashraf Khalil">
       al-Ashraf Khalil
      </a>
      arrived to
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Acre_(1291)" title="Siege of Acre (1291)">
       besiege Acre
      </a>
      in April 1291. Acre was defended by Henry II's brother
      <a href="/wiki/Amalric,_Prince_of_Tyre" title="Amalric, Prince of Tyre">
       Amalric of Tyre
      </a>
      , the Hospitallers, Templars, and Teutonic Knights, the Venetians and Pisans, the French garrison led by
      <a href="/wiki/Jean_I_de_Grailly" title="Jean I de Grailly">
       Jean I de Grailly
      </a>
      , and the English garrison led by
      <a href="/wiki/Otton_de_Grandson" title="Otton de Grandson">
       Otton de Grandson
      </a>
      , but they were vastly outnumbered. Henry II himself arrived in May during the siege, but the city fell on May 18. Henry, Amalric, Otton, and Jean escaped, as did a young Templar named
      <a href="/wiki/Roger_de_Flor" title="Roger de Flor">
       Roger de Flor
      </a>
      , but most of the other defenders did not, including the master of the Templars
      <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_de_Beaujeu" title="Guillaume de Beaujeu">
       Guillaume de Beaujeu
      </a>
      . Tyre fell without a fight the next day, Sidon fell in June, and Beirut in July.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The crusaders moved their headquarters north to cities such as Tortosa, but lost that too, and were forced to relocate their headquarters offshore to Cyprus. Some naval raids and attempts to retake territory were made over the next ten years, but with the loss of the island of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Ruad" title="Siege of Ruad">
       Arwad
      </a>
      in 1302/1303, the Kingdom of Jerusalem ceased to exist on the mainland. The kings of Cyprus for many decades hatched plans to regain the Holy Land, but without success. For the next seven centuries, up to today, a veritable multitude of European monarchs have used the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Kings_of_Jerusalem#Claimants_to_the_throne_of_Jerusalem" title="List of Kings of Jerusalem">
       title of King of Jerusalem
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Life_in_the_early_kingdom">
       Life in the early kingdom
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Life in the early kingdom">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Latins" title="Latins">
       Latin
      </a>
      population of the kingdom was always small; although a steady stream of settlers and new crusaders continually arrived, most of the original crusaders who fought in the First Crusade simply went home. According to
      <a href="/wiki/William_of_Tyre" title="William of Tyre">
       William of Tyre
      </a>
      , "barely three hundred knights and two thousand foot soldiers could be found" in the kingdom in 1100 during Godfrey's siege of
      <a href="/wiki/Arsuf" title="Arsuf">
       Arsuf
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From the very beginning, the Latins were little more than a colonial frontier exercising rule over the native Muslim, Greek and Syrian population, who were more numerous. But
      <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
       Jerusalem
      </a>
      came to be known as
      <a href="/wiki/Outremer" title="Outremer">
       Outremer
      </a>
      , the French word for "overseas", and as new generations grew up in the kingdom, they began to think of themselves as natives, rather than immigrants. Although they never gave up their core identity as Western Europeans or
      <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">
       Franks
      </a>
      , their clothing, diet, and commercialism integrated much Oriental, particularly Byzantine, influence. As the chronicler
      <a href="/wiki/Fulcher_of_Chartres" title="Fulcher of Chartres">
       Fulcher of Chartres
      </a>
      wrote around 1124,
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       "For we who were Occidentals now have been made Orientals. He who was a Roman or Frank has in this land been made into a Galilean or a Palestinian. He who was of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rheims" title="Rheims">
        Rheims
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Chartres" title="Chartres">
        Chartres
       </a>
       has now become a citizen of Tyre or Antioch. We have already forgotten the places of our birth; already these are unknown to many of us or not mentioned any more."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         85
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The crusaders and their descendants often learned to speak
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
       Arabic
      </a>
      , and other eastern languages, and intermarried with the native Christians (whether Greek, Syrian, or Armenian) and sometimes with converted Muslims.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nonetheless, the Frankish principalities remained a distinctive Occidental colony in the heart of Islam.
     </p>
     <p>
      Fulcher, a participant in the First Crusade and chaplain of Baldwin I, continued his chronicle up to 1127. Fulcher's chronicle was very popular and was used as a source by other historians in the west, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Orderic_Vitalis" title="Orderic Vitalis">
       Orderic Vitalis
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/William_of_Malmesbury" title="William of Malmesbury">
       William of Malmesbury
      </a>
      . Almost as soon as Jerusalem had been captured, and continuing throughout the 12th century, many pilgrims arrived and left accounts of the new kingdom; among them are the English Saewulf, the Russian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daniel_Kievsky" title="Daniel Kievsky">
       Abbot Daniel
      </a>
      , the Frank Fretellus, the Byzantine Johannes Phocas, and the Germans John of Wrzburg and Theoderich.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Aside from these, thereafter there is no eyewitness to events in Jerusalem until
      <a href="/wiki/William_of_Tyre" title="William of Tyre">
       William of Tyre
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Tyre" title="Archbishop of Tyre">
       archbishop of Tyre
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Officers_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Officers of the Kingdom of Jerusalem">
       chancellor of Jerusalem
      </a>
      , who began writing around 1167 and died around 1184, although he includes much information about the First Crusade and the intervening years from the death of Fulcher to his own time, drawn mainly from the writings of
      <a href="/wiki/Albert_of_Aix" title="Albert of Aix">
       Albert of Aix
      </a>
      and Fulcher himself. From the Muslim perspective, a chief source of information is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Usamah_ibn_Munqidh" title="Usamah ibn Munqidh">
       Usamah ibn Munqidh
      </a>
      , a soldier and frequent ambassador from Damascus to Jerusalem and Egypt, whose memoirs,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kitab_al_i%27tibar" title="Kitab al i'tibar">
        Kitab al i'tibar
       </a>
      </i>
      , include lively accounts of crusader society in the east. Further information can be gathered from travellers such as
      <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_of_Tudela" title="Benjamin of Tudela">
       Benjamin of Tudela
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Jubayr" title="Ibn Jubayr">
       Ibn Jubayr
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Crusader_society_and_demographics">
       Crusader society and demographics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Crusader society and demographics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crusader_coin_Acre_1230.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1040" data-file-width="1052" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Crusader_coin_Acre_1230.jpg/220px-Crusader_coin_Acre_1230.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Crusader_coin_Acre_1230.jpg/330px-Crusader_coin_Acre_1230.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Crusader_coin_Acre_1230.jpg/440px-Crusader_coin_Acre_1230.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crusader_coin_Acre_1230.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Crusaders coin, Acre, 1230.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crusader_coin_Acre_circa_1230.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1059" data-file-width="1048" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Crusader_coin_Acre_circa_1230.jpg/220px-Crusader_coin_Acre_circa_1230.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Crusader_coin_Acre_circa_1230.jpg/330px-Crusader_coin_Acre_circa_1230.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Crusader_coin_Acre_circa_1230.jpg/440px-Crusader_coin_Acre_circa_1230.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crusader_coin_Acre_circa_1230.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Crusaders coin, Acre, circa 1230.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Kingdom at first was virtually bereft of a loyal subject population and had few
      <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">
       knights
      </a>
      to implement the laws and orders of the realm. With the arrival of Italian trading firms, the creation of the military orders, and immigration by European knights, artisans, and farmers, the affairs of the Kingdom improved and a
      <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">
       feudal
      </a>
      society developed, similar to but distinct from the society the crusaders knew in Europe. The nature of this society has long been a subject of debate among crusade historians.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the 19th and early 20th centuries, French scholars, such as E. G. Rey, Gaston Dodu, and
      <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Grousset" title="Ren Grousset">
       Ren Grousset
      </a>
      believed that the crusaders and the native Muslims and Christians lived in a totally integrated society. Ronnie Ellenblum claims this view was influenced by French imperialism and colonialism; if medieval French crusaders could integrate themselves into local society, then certainly modern French colonies in the Levant could thrive.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the mid-20th century, scholars such as
      <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Prawer" title="Joshua Prawer">
       Joshua Prawer
      </a>
      , R. C. Smail,
      <a href="/wiki/Meron_Benvenisti" title="Meron Benvenisti">
       Meron Benvenisti
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Claude_Cahen" title="Claude Cahen">
       Claude Cahen
      </a>
      argued instead that the crusaders lived totally segregated from the native inhabitants, who were thoroughly Arabicized and/or Islamicized and were a constant threat to the foreign crusaders. Prawer argued further that the kingdom was an early attempt at colonization, in which the crusaders were a small ruling class, who were dependent on the native population for survival but made no attempt to integrate with them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For this reason, the rural European society to which the crusaders were accustomed was replaced by a more secure urban society in the pre-existing cities of the Levant.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to Ellenblum's interpretation the inhabitants of the Kingdom (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" title="Roman Catholicism">
       Latin Christians
      </a>
      living alongside native
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox" title="Greek Orthodox">
       Greek
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Orthodox" title="Syrian Orthodox">
       Syrian Christians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">
       Shia
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">
       Sunni
      </a>
      Arabs,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">
       Sufis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">
       Bedouin
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">
       Turks
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">
       Druze
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
       Jews
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Samaritans" title="Samaritans">
       Samaritans
      </a>
      ) all had major differences between each other as well as with the crusaders. Relations between eastern Christians and the Latin crusaders were "complex and ambiguous", not simply friendly or hostile. The Turks were the common enemy for everyone, as they were only very recent arrivals in the Levant, and although they had imposed their rule prior to the arrival of the crusaders, it is unlikely that they were thoroughly Islamicized as Prawer and others believed. The eastern Christians, at least, probably felt closer ties to their fellow Christian crusaders than to either Turkic overlords or Muslim Arabs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although the crusaders came upon an ancient urban society, Ellenblum argues that they neither completely abandoned their rural European lifestyle, nor was European society completely rural to begin with. Crusader settlement in the Levant resembled the types of colonization and settlement that were already being practiced in Europe, a mixture of urban and rural civilization centred around fortresses. The crusaders were neither totally integrated with the native population, nor did they segregate themselves in the cities away from the rural natives, but rather that they settled in both urban and rural areas; specifically, they settled in areas that had traditionally been inhabited by the eastern Christians. Areas that were traditionally Muslim had very little crusader settlement, just as they already had very few native Christian inhabitants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Into this mixed society the crusaders adapted existing institutions and introduced their own familiar customs from Europe. As in Europe the nobles had their own vassals and were themselves vassals to the king. Agricultural production was regulated by the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iqta" title="Iqta">
        iqta
       </a>
      </i>
      , a Muslim system of land ownership and payments roughly (though far from exactly) equivalent to the feudal system of Europe, and this system was not heavily disrupted by the crusaders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As Hans Mayer says, "the Muslim inhabitants of the Latin Kingdom hardly ever appear in the Latin chronicles", so information on their role in society is difficult to find. The crusaders "had a natural tendency to ignore these matters as simply without interest and certainly not worthy of record."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although Muslims, as well as Jews and Eastern Christians, had virtually no rights in the countryside, where they were essentially the property of the crusader lord who owned the land,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      tolerance for other faiths was in general no higher or lower than that found elsewhere in the Middle East. Greeks, Syrians, and Jews continued to live as they had before, subject to their own laws and courts, with their former Muslim overlords simply replaced by the crusaders; Muslims now joined them at the lowest level of society. The
      <i>
       ra'is
      </i>
      , the leader of a Muslim or Syrian community, was a kind of vassal to whatever noble owned his land, but as the crusader nobles were absentee landlords the
      <i>
       ra'is
      </i>
      and their communities had a high degree of autonomy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
