        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_people" title="Arab people">
       Arab
      </a>
      -
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">
       Andalusian
      </a>
      geographer and traveler
      <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Jubayr" title="Ibn Jubayr">
       Ibn Jubayr
      </a>
      , who was hostile to the Franks, described the Muslims living under the Christian crusaders' Kingdom of Jerusalem in the late 12th-century:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       We left
       <a href="/wiki/Toron" title="Toron">
        Tibnin
       </a>
       by a road running past farms where Muslims live who do very well under the Franks-may Allah preserve us from such a temptation! The regulations imposed on them are the handing over of half of the grain crop at the time of harvest and the payment of a poll tax of one dinar and seven qirats, together with a light duty on their fruit trees. The Muslims own their own houses and rule themselves in their own way. This is the way the farms and big villages are organized in Frankish territory. Many Muslims are sorely tempted to settle here when they see the far from comfortable conditions in which their brethren live in the districts under Muslim rule. Unfortunately for the Muslims, they have always reason for complaint about the injustices of their chiefs in the lands governed by their coreligionists, whereas they can have nothing but praise for the conduct of the Franks, whose justice they can always rely on.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crusaders_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-crusaders-97">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         97
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      In the cities, Muslims and Eastern Christians were free, although no Muslims were permitted to live in Jerusalem itself. They were second-class citizens and played no part in politics or law, and owed no military service to the crown, although in some cities they may have been the majority of the population. Likewise, citizens of the Italian city-states owed nothing as they lived in autonomous quarters in the port cities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There were an unknown number of Muslim
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_medieval_Europe" title="Slavery in medieval Europe">
       slaves
      </a>
      living in the Kingdom. There was a very large slave market in Acre which functioned throughout the twelfth and thirteenth centuries. Although Christians, both Western and Eastern, were by law prohibited from being sold into slavery, the native Christians were often indistinguishable from the Muslim population and the Italian merchants were sometimes accused of selling them along with Muslim slaves.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Slavery was less common than ransom, especially for prisoners of war; the large numbers of prisoners taken during raids and battles every year ensured that ransom money flowed freely between the Christian and Muslim states.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Escape for prisoners and slaves was probably not difficult, as the inhabitants of the countryside were majority Muslim, and fugitive slaves were always a problem. The only legal means of manumission was conversion to (Catholic) Christianity. No Christian, whether Western or Eastern, was permitted by law to be sold into slavery.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The nomadic Bedouin tribes were considered to be the property of the king and under his protection. They could be sold or alienated just like any other property, and later in the 12th century they were often under the protection of a lesser noble or one of the military orders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      21st century positions on the question of cultural integration or cultural apartheid remains debated. Interactions between the Franks and the native Muslims and Christians, though muddled, prevailed on practical coexistence. Though likely overstated, the accounts of Usamah Ibn-Munqidh of Shaizars travels through Antioch and Jerusalem described a level of aristocratic exchange elevated above ethnic prejudice.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Contact between Muslims and Christians came on the administrative or personal level (on the basis of taxes or translation), not communal or cultural, representative of a hierarchical lord over subject relationship.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Evidence of inter-cultural integration remains scarce, but evidence of inter-cultural cooperation and complex social interaction proves more common. Key use of the word dragoman, literally translator, with Syrian administrators and Arabic headsmen represented the direct need for negotiation of interests on both sides.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Comments on households with Arabic-speaking Christians and a few Arabized Jews and Muslims represent a less dichotomous relationship than the mid-20th-century historians depicted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyerman.2C_God.E2.80.99s_War.2C_pg_235_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tyerman.2C_God.E2.80.99s_War.2C_pg_235-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rather, the commonality of Frankish Christians having non-Frankish priests, doctors, and other roles within households and inter-cultural communities presents the lack of standardized discrimination.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyerman.2C_God.E2.80.99s_War.2C_pg_235_106-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tyerman.2C_God.E2.80.99s_War.2C_pg_235-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jersulamite William of Tyre complained about a trend to hire Jewish or Muslim medical practitioners over their Latin and Frankish counterparts. Evidence even indicates alterations to Frankish cultural and social customs regarding hygiene (notorious amongst Arabs for their lack of washing and knowledge of bathhouse culture), going so far as to ensure water supplies for domestic use in addition to irrigation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Population">
       Population
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Population">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      It is impossible to give an accurate estimate of the population of the kingdom. Josiah Russell calculates that all of Syria had about 2.3 million people at the time of the crusades, with perhaps eleven thousand villages; most of these, of course, were outside of crusader rule even at the greatest extent of all four crusader states.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been estimated by scholars such as
      <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Prawer" title="Joshua Prawer">
       Joshua Prawer
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Meron_Benvenisti" title="Meron Benvenisti">
       Meron Benvenisti
      </a>
      that there were at most 120,000 Franks and 100,000 Muslims living in the cities, with another 250,000 Muslim and Eastern Christian peasants in the countryside. The crusaders accounted for 1525% of the total population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Z._Kedar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Benjamin Z. Kedar (page does not exist)">
       Benjamin Z. Kedar
      </a>
      estimates that there were between three hundred thousand and three hundred and sixty thousand non-Franks in the Kingdom, two hundred and fifty thousand of whom were villagers in the countryside, and "one may assume that Muslims were in the majority in some, possibly most parts of the kingdom of Jerusalem"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ronnie_Ellenblum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ronnie Ellenblum (page does not exist)">
       Ronnie Ellenblum
      </a>
      points out, there simply is not enough existing evidence to accurately count the population and any estimate is inherently unreliable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Contemporary chronicler William of Tyre recorded the census of 1183, which was intended to determine the number of men available to defend against an invasion, and to determine the amount of tax money that could be obtained from the inhabitants, Muslim or Christian. If the population was actually counted, William did not record the number.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 13th century,
      <a href="/wiki/John_of_Ibelin_(jurist)" title="John of Ibelin (jurist)">
       John of Ibelin
      </a>
      drew up a list of fiefs and the number of knights owed by each, but this gives no indication of the non-noble, non-Latin population.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Mamluks, led by Baibars, eventually made good their pledge to cleanse the entire Middle East of the Franks. With the fall of
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Antioch_(1268)" title="Siege of Antioch (1268)">
       Antioch
      </a>
      (1268),
      <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Tripoli_(1289)" title="Fall of Tripoli (1289)">
       Tripoli
      </a>
      (1289), and
      <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Acre_(1291)" title="Siege of Acre (1291)">
       Acre
      </a>
      (1291), those Christians unable to leave the cities were massacred or enslaved and the last traces of Christian rule in the Levant disappeared.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
       Economy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Economy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crusader_coins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="1189" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Crusader_coins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem.jpg/350px-Crusader_coins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Crusader_coins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem.jpg/525px-Crusader_coins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Crusader_coins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem.jpg/700px-Crusader_coins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem.jpg 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crusader_coins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Crusader coins of the Kingdom of Jerusalem. Left:
        <a href="/wiki/French_denier" title="French denier">
         Denier
        </a>
        in European style with
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Sepulchre" title="Holy Sepulchre">
         Holy Sepulchre
        </a>
        (116275). Center:
        <a href="/wiki/Kufic" title="Kufic">
         Kufic
        </a>
        gold
        <a href="/wiki/Bezant" title="Bezant">
         bezant
        </a>
        (114080). Right: gold bezant with Christian symbol (1250s). Gold coins were first copied dinars and bore Kufic script, but after 1250
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_symbols" title="Christian symbols">
         Christian symbols
        </a>
        were added following Papal complaints (British Museum).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The urban composition of the area, combined with the presence of the Italian merchants, led to the development of an economy that was much more commercial than it was agricultural.
      <a href="/wiki/Palestine" title="Palestine">
       Palestine
      </a>
      had always been a crossroads for trade; now, this trade extended to Europe as well. European goods, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">
       woolen
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">
       textiles
      </a>
      of northern Europe, made their way to the Middle East and Asia, while Asian goods were transported back to Europe. Jerusalem was especially involved in the silk, cotton and spice trade; other items that first appeared in Europe through trade with crusader Jerusalem included oranges and sugar, the latter of which chronicler William of Tyre called "very necessary for the use and health of mankind." In the countryside, wheat, barley, legumes, olives, grapes, and dates were grown. The Italian city-states made enormous profits from this trade, thanks to commercial treaties like the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pactum_Warmundi" title="Pactum Warmundi">
        Pactum Warmundi
       </a>
      </i>
      , and it influenced their
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
       Renaissance
      </a>
      in later centuries.
     </p>
     <p>
      Jerusalem collected money through tribute payments, first from the coastal cities which had not yet been captured, and later from other neighbouring states such as Damascus and Egypt, which the crusaders could not conquer directly. After Baldwin I extended his rule over Oultrejordain, Jerusalem gained revenue from the taxation of Muslim
      <a href="/wiki/Camel_train" title="Camel train">
       caravans
      </a>
      passing from Syria to Egypt or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">
       Arabia
      </a>
      . The money economy of Jerusalem meant that their manpower problem could be partially solved by paying for
      <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">
       mercenaries
      </a>
      , an uncommon occurrence in medieval Europe. Mercenaries could be fellow European crusaders, or, perhaps more often, Muslim soldiers, including the famous
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turcopoles" title="Turcopoles">
       Turcopoles
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
       Education
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Education">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Holy_sepulchre_exterior.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="800" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Holy_sepulchre_exterior.jpg/240px-Holy_sepulchre_exterior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Holy_sepulchre_exterior.jpg/360px-Holy_sepulchre_exterior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Holy_sepulchre_exterior.jpg/480px-Holy_sepulchre_exterior.jpg 2x" width="240"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Holy_sepulchre_exterior.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Main entrance to the Church of the Holy Sepulchre.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
       Jerusalem
      </a>
      was the center of education in the kingdom. There was a school in the Church of the Holy Sepulchre, where the basic skills of reading and writing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">
       Latin
      </a>
      were taught;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the relative wealth of the merchant class meant that their children could be educated there along with the children of
      <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">
       nobles
      </a>
       it is likely that William of Tyre was a classmate of future king
      <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_III_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin III of Jerusalem">
       Baldwin III
      </a>
      . Higher education had to be undertaken at one of the
      <a href="/wiki/Medieval_university" title="Medieval university">
       universities in Europe
      </a>
      ;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the development of a university was impossible in the culture of
      <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">
       crusader
      </a>
      Jerusalem, where warfare was far more important than philosophy or theology. Nonetheless, the nobility and general Frankish population were noted for the high literacy: lawyers and clerks were in abundance, and the study of law, history, and other academic subjects was a beloved pastime of the royal family and the nobility.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jerusalem had an extensive library not only of ancient and medieval Latin works but of Arabic literature, much of which was apparently captured from Usamah ibn Munqidh and his entourage after a shipwreck in 1154.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Holy Sepulchre contained the kingdom's
      <a href="/wiki/Scriptorium" title="Scriptorium">
       scriptorium
      </a>
      and the city had a
      <a href="/wiki/Chancery_(medieval_office)" title="Chancery (medieval office)">
       chancery
      </a>
      where royal charters and other documents were produced. Aside from Latin, the standard written language of medieval Europe, the populace of crusader Jerusalem communicated in vernacular forms of French and Italian; Greek, Armenian, and even Arabic were used by Frankish settlers.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">
       Art and architecture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Melisende-Psalter_f9v.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="631" data-file-width="436" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Melisende-Psalter_f9v.jpg/220px-Melisende-Psalter_f9v.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Melisende-Psalter_f9v.jpg/330px-Melisende-Psalter_f9v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Melisende-Psalter_f9v.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Melisende-Psalter_f9v.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Melisende_Psalter" title="Melisende Psalter">
         Melisende Psalter
        </a>
        Folio 9v - The Harrowing of Hell
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In Jerusalem itself the greatest architectural endeavour was the expansion of the
      <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">
       Church of the Holy Sepulchre
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">
       western Gothic style
      </a>
      . This expansion consolidated all the separate shrines on the site into one building, and was completed by 1149. Outside of Jerusalem, castles and fortresses were the major focus of construction:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerak" title="Kerak">
       Kerak
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Montreal_(Crusader_castle)" title="Montreal (Crusader castle)">
       Montreal
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Oultrejordain" title="Oultrejordain">
       Oultrejordain
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ibelin" title="Ibelin">
       Ibelin
      </a>
      near
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jaffa,_Israel" title="Jaffa, Israel">
       Jaffa
      </a>
      are among the numerous examples of crusader castles.
     </p>
     <p>
      Crusader art was a mix of
      <a href="/wiki/Medieval_art" title="Medieval art">
       Western
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">
       Byzantine
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">
       Islamic
      </a>
      styles. The major cities featured baths, interior plumbing, and other advanced hygienic tools which were lacking in most other cities and towns throughout the world. The foremost example of crusader art are perhaps the
      <a href="/wiki/Melisende_Psalter" title="Melisende Psalter">
       Melisende Psalter
      </a>
      , an
      <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">
       illuminated manuscript
      </a>
      commissioned between 1135 and 1143 and now located in the
      <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">
       British Library
      </a>
      , and the sculpted
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nazareth_Capitals&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nazareth Capitals (page does not exist)">
       Nazareth Capitals
      </a>
      . Paintings and mosaics were popular forms of art in the kingdom, but many of these were destroyed by the
      <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">
       Mamluks
      </a>
      in the 13th century; only the most durable fortresses survived the reconquest.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_legal_system">
       Government and legal system
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Government and legal system">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Immediately after the First Crusade, land was distributed to loyal vassals of Godfrey, forming numerous
      <a href="/wiki/Vassals_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Vassals of the Kingdom of Jerusalem">
       feudal lordships
      </a>
      within the kingdom. This was continued by Godfrey's successors. The number and importance of the lordships varied throughout the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, and many cities were part of the royal domain. The king was assisted by a number of
      <a href="/wiki/Officers_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Officers of the Kingdom of Jerusalem">
       officers of state
      </a>
      . The king and the royal court were normally located in Jerusalem, but due to the prohibition on Muslim inhabitants, the capital was small and underpopulated. The king just as often held court at Acre,
      <a href="/wiki/Nablus" title="Nablus">
       Nablus
      </a>
      , Tyre, or wherever else he happened to be. In Jerusalem, the royal family lived firstly on the
      <a href="/wiki/Temple_Mount" title="Temple Mount">
       Temple Mount
      </a>
      , before the foundation of the
      <a href="/wiki/Knights_Templar" title="Knights Templar">
       Knights Templar
      </a>
      , and later in the palace complex surrounding the
      <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_David" title="Tower of David">
       Tower of David
      </a>
      ; there was another palace complex in Acre.
     </p>
     <p>
      Because the nobles tended to live in Jerusalem rather than on estates in the countryside, they had a larger influence on the king than they would have had in Europe. The nobles, along with the bishops, formed the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Haute_Cour_of_Jerusalem" title="Haute Cour of Jerusalem">
        haute cour
       </a>
      </i>
      (high court), which was responsible for confirming the election of a new king (or a regent if necessary), collecting taxes, minting coins, allotting money to the king, and raising armies. The
      <i>
       haute cour
      </i>
      was the only judicial body for the nobles of the kingdom, hearing criminal cases such as murder, rape, and treason, and simpler feudal disputes such as recovery of slaves, sales and purchases of
      <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">
       fiefs
      </a>
      , and default of service. Punishments included forfeiture of land and exile, or in extreme cases death. The first laws of the kingdom were, according to tradition, established during Godfrey of Bouillon's short reign, but were more probably established by Baldwin II at the
      <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Nablus" title="Council of Nablus">
       Council of Nablus
      </a>
      in 1120. Benjamin Z. Kedar argued that the canons of the Council of Nablus were in force in the 12th century but had fallen out of use by the thirteenth. Marwan Nader questions this and suggests that the canons may not have applied to the whole kingdom at all times.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most extensive collection of laws, together known as
      <a href="/wiki/Assizes_of_Jerusalem" title="Assizes of Jerusalem">
       Assizes of Jerusalem
      </a>
      , were written in the mid-13th century, although many of them are purported to be twelfth-century in origin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There were other, lesser courts for non-nobles and non-Latins; the
      <i>
       Cour des Bourgeois
      </i>
      provided justice for non-noble Latins, dealing with minor criminal offences such as assault and theft, and provided rules for disputes between non-Latins, who had fewer legal rights. Special courts such as the
      <i>
       Cour de la Fond
      </i>
      (for commercial disputes in the markets) and the
      <i>
       Cour de la Mer
      </i>
      (an
      <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">
       admiralty
      </a>
      court) existed in the coastal cities. The extent to which native Islamic and Eastern Christian courts continued to function is unknown, but the
      <i>
       ra'is
      </i>
      probably exercised some legal authority on a local level. The
      <i>
       Cour des Syriens
      </i>
      judged non-criminal matters among the native Christians (the "Syrians"). For criminal matters non-Latins were to be tried in the
      <i>
       Cour des Bourgeois
      </i>
      (or even the
      <i>
       Haute Cour
      </i>
      if the crime was sufficiently severe).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Italian communes were granted almost complete autonomy from the very early days of the Kingdom, thanks to their military and naval support in the years following the First Crusade. This autonomy included the right to administer their own justice, although the kinds of cases that fell under their jurisdiction varied at different times.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The king was recognised as head of the Haute Cour, although he was legally only
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">
        primus inter pares
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Arms_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem">
       Arms of the Kingdom of Jerusalem
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Arms of the Kingdom of Jerusalem">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="floatleft">
      <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Armoiries_de_J%C3%A9rusalem.svg">
       <img alt="Armoiries de Jrusalem.svg" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="600" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Armoiries_de_J%C3%A9rusalem.svg/100px-Armoiries_de_J%C3%A9rusalem.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Armoiries_de_J%C3%A9rusalem.svg/150px-Armoiries_de_J%C3%A9rusalem.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Armoiries_de_J%C3%A9rusalem.svg/200px-Armoiries_de_J%C3%A9rusalem.svg.png 2x" width="100"/>
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">
       coat of arms
      </a>
      of the Kingdom of Jerusalem, which has gone through several different varieties of a cross
      <i>
       Or
      </i>
      (gold) on an
      <i>
       argent
      </i>
      (silver) field, is a famous violation of or exception to the
      <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_tincture" title="Rule of tincture">
       rule of tincture
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">
       heraldry
      </a>
      , which prohibits the placement of metal on metal.
     </p>
     <p>
      It is one of the earliest known coats of arms. The main cross is a
      <i>
       cross potent
      </i>
      (called a
      <i>
       Jerusalem cross
      </i>
      for its use here),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      whereas the smaller crosses are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_cross" title="Greek cross">
       Greek crosses
      </a>
      , one of the many Byzantine influences on the kingdom.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Later_claimants_to_the_Throne_of_Jerusalem">
       Later claimants to the Throne of Jerusalem
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Jerusalem&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Later claimants to the Throne of Jerusalem">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/King_of_Jerusalem" title="King of Jerusalem">
       King of Jerusalem
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      After the fall of the Kingdom of Jerusalem, various European dynasties and royal houses claimed its throne and formally registered it among their titles, though none of them made any attempt to actually regain rule over its territory. At present, claimants include King
      <a href="/wiki/Felipe_VI_of_Spain" title="Felipe VI of Spain">
       Felipe VI of Spain
      </a>
      as well as several claimants to European thrones in countries where the monarchy had been abolished.
     </p>

         </i>
         , ed. R. B. C. Huygens, Corpus Christianorum, Continuatio Medievalis, vol. 38 (Turnhout: Brepols, 1986), bk. 19, ch. 12, pp. 879881. This chapter was discovered after the publication of Babcock and Krey's translation and is not included in the English edition.

       <li id="cite_note-117">

          <a href="#cite_ref-117">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         For example, King Baldwin III "was fairly well educated", and "particularly enjoyed listening to the reading of history..." (William of Tyre, vol. 2, bk. 16, ch. 2, pg. 138.) King Amalric I "was fairly well educated, although much less so than his brother" Baldwin III; he "was well skilled in the customary law by which the kingdom was governed", and "listened eagerly to history and preferred it to all other kinds of reading." (William of Tyre, vol. 2, bk. 19, ch. 2, pg. 296.)
